It might be that a1 must be composite and not a prime, and also not equal to any number that leads to a prime or a deficient number (like 4,9,25,49, etc.) within a few steps. The problem: "Determine all possible values of a1." That suggests that the set of possible a1 is some specific set (maybe all multiples of 6? but 12 is multiple of 6 and fails). Let's explore the function f(N) more systematically. Let's denote N's prime factorization. We need to compute the three largest proper divisors. Equivalent to: The three largest proper divisors are N divided by the three smallest divisors > 1 (including 1?). Actually the largest proper divisor is N / p_min. The second largest proper divisor is N / d where d is the second smallest divisor > 1. But careful: If N has many divisors, the third largest proper divisor is N / d where d is the third smallest divisor > 1. However is it always true that the three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors > 1? Let's confirm with example: N=12, divisors ascending: 1,2,3,4,6,12. Smallest divisors >1: 2,3,4. Then N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3. Indeed those are the three largest proper divisors (6,4,3). So that holds. For N=18, divisors: 1,2,3,6,9,18. Smallest >1: 2,3,6. N/2=9, N/3=6, N/6=3. The three largest proper divisors are 9,6,3 (sum 18). So indeed they are N/p, N/q, N/r where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1. However note that if N is a square, then the divisor sqrt(N) appears, and it is the middle one. For N=36, divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,9,12,18,36. Smallest >1: 2,3,4. Then N/2=18, N/3=12, N/4=9. Those are the three largest proper divisors (18,12,9). That matches. So the mapping is correct. So we can compute f(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4 where d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1 (i.e., the smallest three proper divisors, but not including 1). However note that if N has only two proper divisors, then d4 would not exist. So we assume N has at least three proper divisors. So d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1.

Thus f(N) = N * (1/d2 + 1/d3 + 1/d4). Since N is composite, these are integers.

Let's attempt a systematic analysis.

Define S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. We'll compute S(N) based on prime factorization.

We need to find all positive integers a1 such that for all n≥1, a_n is composite with at least 3 proper divisors. Since the sequence is defined recursively, this is a property of the orbit. So we need to characterize the set of "good" numbers (those from which the sequence stays good). Let's denote G = { N ∈ ℕ⁺ | the sequence defined by a1 = N, a_{n+1}=S(a_n) is such that all a_n have at least 3 proper divisors }. Then we need to determine G.

Observations:

1. If N is prime or a square of a prime, then N has at most 2 proper divisors, so N ∉ G (since a1 itself fails). So G ⊆ {composite numbers with at least 3 proper divisors}.

2. If N is a fixed point (S(N)=N), then trivially N ∈ G, because then the sequence is constant N, and N has at least 3 proper divisors (by definition of fixed point we need to ensure N indeed has at least 3 proper divisors; but we will characterize fixed points as numbers N such that S(N)=N and N has at least 3 proper divisors. Actually if S(N)=N, then N must have at least 3 proper divisors (since we can sum three largest, so N has at least 3 proper divisors). So N ∈ G.

3. If N is such that S(N) is a fixed point and N itself has at least 3 proper divisors, then N ∈ G because the orbit becomes constant after one step. However, we must also ensure that S(N) has at least 3 proper divisors (which it does if it's a fixed point). So that's fine. So numbers that map to a fixed point are in G. Similarly, numbers that map to a number that eventually maps to a fixed point (without hitting bad numbers) are also in G, provided all intermediate numbers have at least 3 proper divisors.

Thus the problem reduces to finding all N such that the orbit under S never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This is a kind of "invariant" or "stable" set.

We can try to prove that G is exactly the set of numbers that are multiples of 6 and are not divisible by 4 or 5? But 72 is a multiple of 6 and divisible by 4, yet it's in G. So that's not correct. So fails. 192? 192=2^6*3, S=96+64+48=208. 208=2^4*13 -> S=104+52+26=182? Actually 208/2=104, /4=52, /8=26? Wait need to compute triple: 208's divisors: 1,2,4,8,13,16,26,52,104,208. Smallest >1:2,4,8 => S=104+52+26=182 (fails). So fails. 216=2^3*3^3, S=108+72+54=234. 234=2*3^2*13 -> S=117+78+18? Actually 234/2=117, /3=78, /6=39? Wait need triple: 234's divisors:1,2,3,6,9,13,18,26,39,78,117,234. Smallest >1:2,3,6 => S=117+78+39=234. So 234 is a fixed point? Let's compute: 234/2=117, /3=78, /6=39 => sum=234. Yes, 234 is a fixed point. So 216 maps to 234 (fixed). 216 is allowed. So 216 works. So indeed many numbers with exponent 2 >=2 work as long as after one step they land on a fixed point. But some like 12,24,36,48,60,84,96,108,120,132,144,156,168,180,192, etc fail. So what distinguishes them? Let's analyze S(N) for N that are multiples of 6.

Let N be a multiple of 6. Write N = 2^a * 3^b * c, where c is an integer not divisible by 2 or 3. Then the smallest divisors >1 are determined by a and b.

We need to consider the three smallest divisors >1. The divisors are all numbers of the form 2^i * 3^j * d where d divides c. The smallest ones are those with small i and j. The candidates: 2, 3, 4 (if a≥2), 6, 8 (if a≥3), 9 (if b≥2), etc. So we need to compare values.

Let's list possible triples (d2,d3,d4) for N that are multiples of 6.

Case A: a = 1 (i.e., N has exactly one factor 2). Then N is even but not divisible by 4. Then 2 is a divisor. 3 is a divisor if b≥1 (since N is multiple of 6, b≥1). 4 is not a divisor. So the smallest divisors >1 are 2, 3, and then the next smallest could be 6 (since 6 is a divisor). But also if c has a prime factor p (like 5,7, etc.), then p could be a divisor. But note that p could be less than 6? The smallest possible primes >3 are 5,7,11,... So if c is divisible by 5, then 5 is a divisor, and 5<6. So the triple becomes 2,3,5. If c is not divisible by 5, then 6 is the next smallest divisor. Also if c is divisible by some number like 1? That's trivial. So for a=1, the triple is (2,3,6) iff N is not divisible by any integer d with 1<d<6 except 2 and 3, i.e., not divisible by 4 (already) and not divisible by 5. So that's the fixed point condition. If N is divisible by 5, then triple (2,3,5) and S(N) = N/2+N/3+N/5 = (15N+10N+6N)/30 = 31N/30. So S(N) = 31N/30. That is an integer if N is divisible by 30. Since N is multiple of 6, for N to be divisible by 30, N must be divisible by 5. So N = 30k. Then S(N) = 31k. So indeed.

Thus for a=1, we have subcases:
- N not divisible by 5 => S(N)=N (fixed).
- N divisible by 5 => S(N) = 31N/30 = 31k, where N=30k. Then S(N) may or may not be a fixed point, etc.

Now case B: a ≥ 2 (i.e., N divisible by 4). Then N is divisible by 4. Also N is divisible by 2 and 3, so N is multiple of 12. So N = 12 * t, where t is integer. The smallest divisors >1: 2,3,4. 4 is smaller than 6. So the triple is (2,3,4). Then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12 = 13t. So S(N) = 13t, where N = 12t.

Now, for such N, S(N) may or may not be a fixed point, but we need to check whether the orbit stays good. If S(N) is a fixed point (i.e., S(N) is a multiple of 6 with exponent 1 and not divisible by 5), then N ∈ G. If S(N) is not a fixed point, then we need to examine further.

So the problem reduces to determining which a1 (N) produce an infinite sequence with each term having ≥3 proper divisors. Since the recursion is deterministic, we can analyze the orbit.

Observation: For any N that is a multiple of 6, if we apply S repeatedly, we might get numbers that are also multiples of 6 (maybe not always). For example, N=12 (multiple of 6) gives S=13 (not multiple of 6). So it's possible to leave multiples of 6. For N=72, S=78 which is multiple of 6. For N=216, S=234 which is multiple of 6. For N=30, S=31 (not multiple). So some multiples of 6 map to non-multiples. The condition for the orbit to be good is that all terms are composite with at least 3 proper divisors. So if any term becomes prime or a square of a prime, it fails. Also if any term becomes 1 (impossible). So we need to avoid those.

Now, we might try to characterize the set of numbers that are "bad" (i.e., have <3 proper divisors). Those are primes and squares of primes. The iteration from a good starting value might eventually become constant at a fixed point. Perhaps the only way to avoid a bad number is to have the orbit eventually become a fixed point, and that fixed point must be a number that is not a square of a prime (which is automatically satisfied because fixed points have at least 3 proper divisors). So we need to find all N such that the orbit under S never hits a prime or a prime square. That seems plausible.

Let's attempt to compute S for numbers that are not multiples of 6. Perhaps we can prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then S(N) is either a prime or a prime square, or that after a few steps we hit such. But we saw N=10 (not multiple of 6) gave S=8 which is not prime or prime square, but then 8 leads to 7 (prime). So it's not immediate. However, maybe we can prove that if N is not divisible by 6, then S(N) is less than N? Not necessarily; 8 is 2^3, S(8)=7 < 8. 10 -> 8 < 10. 14 -> 10 < 14. 15 -> 9 < 15. 21 -> 11 < 21. 22 -> 14 < 22. 26 -> 16 < 26. 27 -> 13 < 27. 33 -> 15 < 33. So indeed S(N) seems smaller than N for many numbers. Could there be N not divisible by 6 where S(N) > N? Let's test N=4 (prime square) has only 2 proper divisors, S not defined. N=9 has only 2 proper divisors, S not defined. N=25 has only 2 proper divisors. So not considered. For composite numbers with at least 3 proper divisors, if they are not multiples of 6, they could be powers of 2 (8,16,32,64,...) which are multiples of 2 but not of 3. For 2^k with k≥3, S = 2^{k-1}+2^{k-2}+2^{k-3}. That's less than 2^k. For numbers like 2*5=10, S=8 <10. 2*7=14, S=10 <14. 2*11=22, S=14 <22. 2*13=26, S=16 <26. So seems S(N) < N for numbers not divisible by 6? Let's test N=2*3*5=30, which is divisible by 6, but S=31 >30. So that's an exception. So numbers not divisible by 6 have S(N) < N. But is it always true? Consider N=3*5=15 (divisible by 3 but not 2), S=9 <15. 3*7=21, S=11 <21. 3*11=33, S=15 <33. 5*7=35 (not divisible by 2 or 3), S=13 <35. So yes. For numbers that are multiples of 2 but not 3, S(N) is less than N (since N/2 + N/4 + N/6? Wait careful: if N is even but not multiple of 3, then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2, and then perhaps something else like 4, and then 5? Actually need to compute. For N even but not divisible by 3, N may be divisible by 2 and possibly 4,5, etc. The smallest divisors >1 are 2, then 4 (if N is divisible by 4), then maybe 5 or 6? But 6 is not a divisor because not divisible by 3. So the triple will be (2,4,? or 2, something, something). But we need to compare S(N) with N. For N=2^3=8, S=7<8. For N=2^4=16, S=14<16. For N=2^5=32, S=28<32. For N=2^6=64, S=56<64. So indeed S(N) < N. For N=2*5=10, S=8<10. For N=2*7=14, S=10<14. For N=2*11=22, S=14<22. For N=2*13=26, S=16<26. So it seems that for numbers not divisible by 6, S(N) < N. But is it possible that a number not divisible by 6 has S(N) > N? Let's examine N=2*3*5? That's divisible by 6. So not considered. N=2*3*7? That's divisible by 6. So indeed any number that is not a multiple of 6 is either not even or not divisible by 3. But if it is not even, it's odd. Then its smallest divisor >1 is an odd prime p. For odd composite numbers with at least 3 proper divisors, like 35 (5*7) is odd, not divisible by 3. Its divisors: 1,5,7,35. The three largest proper divisors are 7,5,1 sum=13 <35. So S(N) < N. For numbers like 9 (3^2) fails because only 2 proper divisors. So indeed S(N) < N for all N that are not multiples of 6 and have at least 3 proper divisors. But we should prove this.

Similarly, for numbers that are multiples of 6 but not fixed points, S(N) can be larger or smaller? For N=12, S=13>12. For N=30, S=31>30. For N=36, S=39>36. For N=48, S=52>48. For N=60, S=65>60. Now, the sequence for a1 that is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point will increase (strictly) as long as the term remains a multiple of 6 and not a fixed point. Perhaps the answer is: all a1 that are multiples of 6 and are not divisible by 5? But 12 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, yet fails. So that's not sufficient.

Maybe the answer is: all a1 that are multiples of 6 and also are not multiples of 5? That's false. So need a more refined condition.

Let's explore the function S for numbers that are multiples of 6 but not fixed points. Write N = 6 * k. Then we need to compute S(N) based on whether N is divisible by 4 or 5. But we can derive formulas:

If N is divisible by 4 (i.e., exponent of 2 >= 2), then N = 12 * t, S(N) = 13 t.

If N is divisible by 5 but not by 4 (i.e., exponent of 2 = 1, and N is divisible by 5), then N = 30 * s, S(N) = 31 s.

If N is divisible by 4 and 5? That's N divisible by 60, then both conditions hold. For N = 60, which is 12*5, S = 13*5 = 65. That's also 30*2, S = 31*2 = 62? So for N that is a multiple of 6, we have:

- Let a = exponent of 2 in N. Then if a = 1, the candidates are 2,3,6, and also any divisor from c (primes) that are <6, i.e., 5. So if N is divisible by 5, then the third smallest is 5; else it's 6. So S(N) = N/2+N/3+N/5 if 5 divides N (and a=1), else S(N)=N. But also if a >=2, then 4 is a divisor, so the triple is (2,3,4) regardless of divisibility by 5 (since 4 < 5). So S(N) = N/2+N/3+N/4 = 13N/12. That is valid if N is divisible by 12. So for a >=2, S(N) = 13N/12. That's always > N.

Thus the behavior depends on the exponent of 2.

Now, the problem is to determine all possible a1. So we need to find all N such that the recursively defined sequence (a_n) satisfies that each a_n has at least 3 proper divisors. Since the recursion is deterministic, we can analyze the possible orbits.

Idea: Prove that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually becomes a prime (or prime square) within a few steps, which violates the condition. So a1 must be a multiple of 6. Then, among multiples of 6, some lead to sequences that never hit a bad number. We need to characterize those.

So we see many numbers fail. But some succeed: 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,162,186,222,234,258, etc. Also 72,216, etc. So the pattern seems to be that numbers that are multiples of 6 and have exponent of 2 = 1 (i.e., not divisible by 4) and also not divisible by 5 are fixed points. But note that 12,24,36,48,60,84,96,108,120,132,144,156,168,180,192,204,210,240,252,264,270 are all multiples of 6. Some of them succeed (72,216). So what's the difference? Let's compare the factorization of numbers that succeed vs those that fail.

Succeed: 6 (2*3), 18 (2*3^2), 42 (2*3*7), 54 (2*3^3), 66 (2*3*11), 78 (2*3*13), 102 (2*3*17), 114 (2*3*19), 126 (2*3^2*7), 162 (2*3^4), 186 (2*3*31), 222 (2*3*37), 234 (2*3^2*13), 258 (2*3*43), 282? Actually 282=2*3*47, fixed. So many fixed points.

Also 72 (2^3*3^2) succeeds; 216 (2^3*3^3) succeeds. So numbers with exponent of 2 >=2 succeed if they are of the form 2^a * 3^b * c where c is odd and not divisible by 5, and also the triple (2,3,4) leads to S(N)=13N/12 = 13 * (N/12). For such N, S(N) is a multiple of 13. For S(N) to be a fixed point, it must be a multiple of 6 with exponent 1 and not divisible by 5. So we need 13 * t (where N=12t) to be a multiple of 6, i.e., 13t divisible by 6, which implies t is divisible by 6 (since 13 is odd). So t must be a multiple of 6. Then S(N) = 13t, and since t is multiple of 6, 13t is also multiple of 6. Moreover, we need S(N) to not be divisible by 4 (i.e., exponent of 2 is 1) and not divisible by 5. So we need to check whether 13t has exponent of 2 = 1 (i.e., is exactly 2 * odd). Since t is multiple of 6, t is even, so t has at least one factor 2. Then 13t will have the same exponent of 2 as t. So for S(N) to be a fixed point, we need t to have exponent of 2 exactly 1, i.e., t is even but not divisible by 4. Also, 13t should not be divisible by 5. So t should not be a multiple of 5. So if N = 12t, with t being a multiple of 6, and t is not divisible by 4 (i.e., exponent of 2 is 1) and t not divisible by 5, then S(N) = 13t will be a fixed point (since it's multiple of 6 with exponent 1 and not divisible by 5). But we also need that S(N) itself has at least 3 proper divisors, which is true for fixed points.

Thus numbers N that are multiples of 12 (i.e., divisible by 4 and 3) and such that N/12 = t is a multiple of 6, and also t is not divisible by 4 or 5. But wait, N/12 = t. For N to be a multiple of 12, t is integer. We want S(N) to be a fixed point. That is, we want 13t to be a fixed point. The condition for 13t to be a fixed point is: 13t is a multiple of 6 (i.e., divisible by 2 and 3) and not divisible by 4 or 5. Since 13 is odd, 13t is divisible by 2 iff t is even. So t must be even. Also, 13t is divisible by 3 iff t is divisible by 3. So t must be divisible by 3. So t is a multiple of 6. So indeed N = 12t with t multiple of 6. That's one condition. Now, does t need to have exponent of 2 = 1? That is, t should be divisible by 2 but not by 4. And also t should not be divisible by 5. So overall, N is a multiple of 12 and N/12 is a multiple of 6 (i.e., N is a multiple of 72? Actually 12 * (multiple of 6) = 12 * 6k = 72k. So N is a multiple of 72. That's consistent: 72, 144? But 144/12=12, which is multiple of 6? 12 is multiple of 6 (12 = 6*2). So 144/12 = 12, which is multiple of 6. But earlier we computed 144 -> 156, then 156 -> 169 fails. So 144 does not lead to a fixed point. Let's compute: N=144, t=12. Then S(N)=13*12=156. 156 is not a fixed point. So we need 13t to be a fixed point, but t=12 is a multiple of 6, but t is divisible by 4? 12 = 2^2 * 3, exponent of 2 is 2. So t is divisible by 4. Then 13t = 13*12 = 156. 156's exponent of 2 is 2 (since 156 = 2^2 * 3 * 13). So 156 is not a fixed point. So indeed for S(N) to be a fixed point, we need 13t to be a fixed point. That requires that 13t has exponent of 2 = 1. Since 13t's exponent of 2 equals t's exponent of 2. So t must have exponent of 2 = 1. So t is even but not divisible by 4. Also, t must not be divisible by 5, because otherwise 13t would be divisible by 5, and we need to check whether a fixed point can be divisible by 5? We argued that fixed points cannot be divisible by 5. So 13t should not be divisible by 5. So t not divisible by 5. Also, t must be a multiple of 6 (for 13t to be multiple of 6). So t is a multiple of 6, but with exponent of 2 exactly 1 (i.e., t ≡ 2 mod 4) and not divisible by 5. So t is of the form 6 * (odd) where the odd is not multiple of 5? Let's analyze. Since t is a multiple of 6, t = 6 * u. Then exponent of 2 in t is at least 1 (since 6 = 2 * 3). If u is odd, then exponent of 2 in t is exactly 1. If u is even, then exponent is at least 2. So we need u to be odd. Also, we need t not divisible by 5, i.e., 6u not divisible by 5, so u not divisible by 5. Thus t = 6 * u with u odd and not divisible by 5. Then N = 12 t = 12 * 6 * u = 72 u. So N is a multiple of 72, but with the additional condition that the odd part u is not divisible by 5. However, we also need that N itself has at least 3 proper divisors. Since N is multiple of 12, it has many divisors. So N ∈ G.

Now, what about N that are multiples of 6 with exponent of 2 = 1 but are divisible by 5? Those fail as we saw. So those are not allowed. So allowed a1 include:

- All multiples of 6 with exponent of 2 = 1 and not divisible by 5 (i.e., fixed points). This set includes numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126, etc. Also includes numbers like 30? No, 30 is divisible by 5, so not allowed. So those are the fixed points.
- Additionally, numbers that are multiples of 72 (i.e., divisible by 12 and also N/12 is a multiple of 6) with the condition that N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But note: N = 72 * u, with u odd and not divisible by 5. Then N is a multiple of 72, so exponent of 2 at least 3 (since 72 = 2^3 * 3^2). So exponent of 2 is at least 3, which is ≥2. So these are not fixed points. But they map to S(N) = 13 * (N/12) = 13 * (72u / 12) = 13 * (6u) = 78 u. Wait compute: N = 72 u. Then N/12 = 6 u. Then S(N) = 13 * (6 u) = 78 u. So S(N) = 78 u. Now, is 78 u a fixed point? 78 u = (2 * 3 * 13) * u. For it to be a fixed point, we need it to be a multiple of 6 with exponent of 2 = 1 and not divisible by 5. The exponent of 2 in 78 u is 1 (since 78 = 2 * 3 * 13). So exponent of 2 is 1. Also, 78 u is divisible by 3. So it is a multiple of 6. For it to be a fixed point, we also need that it is not divisible by 5, i.e., u is not divisible by 5. So indeed if u is odd and not divisible by 5, then 78 u is a fixed point. So such N are allowed. But we also have numbers like 72 (u=1) works. 144 (u=2) fails because u is even, then 78*2=156 which is not a fixed point. But 144 is multiple of 72? 144/72=2, which is even. So indeed 144 fails. So the condition for N that are multiples of 72 is that the quotient N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. That's a subset.

Now, what about numbers that are multiples of 6 with exponent of 2 = 1 but are not divisible by 5 (fixed points). Those are allowed. Now, we can try to deduce contradictions based on the values of d2,d3,d4.

We have S(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4.

Case 1: N is odd → S(N) is odd → not multiple of 6.

Case 2: N is even → write N = 2^a * m with m odd, a ≥ 1. Since N is not a multiple of 6, we have 3 ∤ N. So 3 ∤ m. Also, a could be 1 or ≥2. We'll analyze.

If a ≥ 2, then d2 = 2, d3 = 4, d4 = 8 (if a ≥ 3) or d4 = p (smallest odd prime factor) if a = 2. But we can handle a≥2 with subcases.

Subcase 2a: a ≥ 3 → d4 = 8. Then S(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/8 = (4N+2N+N)/8 = 7N/8. Since N is divisible by 8, 7N/8 is integer. We want to show that 6 ∤ S(N). That is, S(N) is not divisible by 2 and 3. But S(N) may be even or odd. However, we can prove that S(N) is not divisible by 3. As computed, N ≡ ±1 mod 3 → N/2 ≡ 2N, N/4 ≡ N, N/8 ≡ 2N. So S(N) ≡ 2N + N + 2N = 5N ≡ 2N (mod 3). Since N is not 0 mod 3, 2N ≠ 0 mod 3. So S(N) is not divisible by 3. Therefore, S(N) cannot be a multiple of 6. Good.

Subcase 2b: a = 2 → N = 4 * m, with m odd. Then d2 = 2, d3 = 4. d4 is the third smallest divisor >1. Since N is not divisible by 8, the next power of 2 is not a divisor. So d4 is the smallest odd divisor of N (i.e., the smallest prime factor of m). Let's denote p = smallest odd prime factor of m. Then d4 = p. (We need to confirm that there is no smaller divisor after 4; could there be a number like 2p, which is even, but 2p may be less than p? No, 2p > p. So the third smallest is p.) So d4 = p.

Now, S(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/p = 2m + m + (4m)/p = 3m + (4m)/p. Since m = p * t, with t integer. Then N/p = 4t. So S(N) = 3m + 4t = 3p t + 4t = t (3p + 4). We want to show that S(N) is not a multiple of 6. Since S(N) is integer. Compute parity and mod 3.

We have S(N) = t (3p + 4). Since p is odd, 3p is odd, plus 4 gives odd? 3p is odd, +4 = odd+even = odd. So 3p+4 is odd. Therefore, S(N) has the same parity as t. So S(N) is even iff t is even. So S(N) could be even. Now, we need to check whether S(N) can be divisible by 3. S(N) = t (3p+4). Since 3p+4 ≡ (p+1) mod 3? Actually 3p is divisible by 3, so 3p+4 ≡ 4 ≡ 1 (mod 3). So 3p+4 ≡ 1 (mod 3). Therefore, S(N) ≡ t (mod 3). So S(N) is divisible by 3 iff t ≡ 0 (mod 3). So S(N) can be divisible by 3 if t is a multiple of 3. In that case, S(N) is divisible by 3. But we also need S(N) to be even to be a multiple of 6. So if t is even and a multiple of 3, then S(N) would be divisible by 6. So we need to see if such t can occur. t = m/p, where m is odd and not divisible by 3. p is an odd prime factor of m. Could t be even and a multiple of 3? t is integer. Since m is odd, and p is odd, t could be odd or even. For t to be even, m/p must be even. That means m is divisible by 2? But m is odd, so m/p is odd (since odd/odd is odd). Actually if both m and p are odd, then m/p is odd (odd divided by odd is odd). Because odd/odd is odd if it's integer, but careful: odd/odd can be even if the quotient is even? Example: 9/3=3 (odd). 15/5=3 (odd). 21/7=3 (odd). 25/5=5 (odd). 27/3=9 (odd). 33/3=11 (odd). 35/5=7 (odd). 39/3=13 (odd). 45/5=9 (odd). So indeed, quotient of two odd numbers is odd. Because if both are odd, their product is odd; dividing an odd by an odd yields an odd number (since if the quotient were even, the product would be even). More formally, if a = b * c with a,b odd, then c must be odd. So t is odd. Therefore, t is odd. Hence, S(N) is odd (since t odd, 3p+4 odd). So S(N) is odd, thus not a multiple of 6. So subcase 2b yields S(N) odd, not multiple of 6.

Thus, for a ≥ 2, we have S(N) not multiple of 6. So the only potential problematic case is a = 1. So we need to handle a = 1.

Now, a = 1 means N is even but not divisible by 4. Write N = 2 * m, with m odd. Since N is not a multiple of 6, 3 ∤ N, so 3 ∤ m. The three smallest divisors >1 are:

- d2 = 2.
- d3 = p, where p is the smallest odd prime factor of m.
- d4: we need to consider the third smallest divisor >1. Since N is not divisible by 4, the powers of 2 are only 2. So after 2, we have p. Then the next smallest divisor could be either:

   - 2p (if 2p is a divisor, and it's less than any other odd divisor besides p). But note that 2p is even, and it's a divisor because N = 2 * m, and p divides m, so 2p divides N.
   - Another odd divisor q, the second smallest odd prime factor of m, if q < 2p.
   - Also, could there be p^2 if p^2 divides m, but p^2 > 2p for p > 2? Let's compare p^2 and 2p. For p ≥ 3, p^2 - 2p = p(p-2) > 0. So p^2 > 2p. So p^2 is larger than 2p. So the third smallest is min(2p, q). So d4 = min(2p, q), where q is the next odd prime factor of m (if any). If m is a power of p (i.e., m = p^k), then there is no q, so d4 = 2p (since 2p is a divisor). So d4 is either 2p (if m has no other odd prime factors besides p, or if 2p < q) or q (if q < 2p).

Now, we need to prove that S(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/d4 is not a multiple of 6. S(N) may be odd or even, but we need to show that it's not both even and divisible by 3. Let's compute S(N) explicitly in terms of m and p.

N = 2m. N/2 = m. N/p = 2m/p. Since p divides m, write m = p * t, where t is a positive integer. Then N/p = 2t. Also, N/d4 depends on d4.

Case A: d4 = 2p. Then N/d4 = N/(2p) = (2m)/(2p) = m/p = t. So S(N) = m + 2t + t = m + 3t = p t + 3t = t(p + 3). Since m = p t, and m is odd, p is odd, t is odd (odd/odd is odd). So t is odd. p+3 is even (odd+odd = even). So S(N) = odd * even = even. So S(N) is even. Now, S(N) mod 3: t(p+3) ≡ t * (p mod 3 + 0) mod 3. p mod 3 can be 1 or 2. So p+3 ≡ p (mod 3). So S(N) ≡ t * p (mod 3). Since t is odd and p is odd, but mod 3 we need to check if S(N) can be 0. For S(N) to be divisible by 3, we need t p ≡ 0 mod 3. Since 3 ∤ N and N = 2 p t, and 3 ∤ N, we have 3 ∤ (p t). Because if 3 divides p t, then 3 divides N (since N = 2 * p t). So p t ≠ 0 mod 3. Therefore, t p mod 3 ≠ 0. Hence, S(N) is not divisible by 3. So S(N) is even but not a multiple of 3 → not multiple of 6. Good.

Case B: d4 = q, where q is the second smallest odd prime factor of m, and q < 2p. Then we need to compute N/d4 = (2 m)/q = 2 (m/q). Since q divides m, write m = q * r, where r is integer. Then N/d4 = 2 r. Also, note that p is the smallest odd prime factor, so p < q. Also, we need to consider the possibility that m has a factor p^2 and q < 2p but q could be larger than p, but we also need to consider if p^2 appears. But as we have, d4 is q. Now, we also have N/p = 2 m/p = 2 (p t)/p? Actually m = p t. But also m = q r. Since p < q, it's possible that q does not divide m/p? Actually m may have both p and q, but we need to relate. Since m has both p and q as factors, we can write m = p^a * q^b * ... with a,b ≥ 1. But we only know p is smallest. So m = p * u, where u is integer. Then q divides u (since q is a factor of m, and p does not divide q, so q divides u). So u = q * v. Then m = p * q * v. So t = m/p = q * v. So t is not odd necessarily? But t is m/p, and m is odd, p is odd, so t is odd. Since q is odd, v is odd as well (odd/odd is odd). So t is odd. That's consistent. Then N/p = 2t = 2 q v. Now, d4 = q, so N/d4 = (2 m)/q = 2 (p q v)/q = 2 p v. So S(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/d4 = m + 2t + 2 p v = p q v + 2 q v + 2 p v = v ( p q + 2 q + 2 p ). So S(N) = v * ( p q + 2 p + 2 q ). Now, we need to analyze parity and mod 3.

First, note that t = q v, and t is odd, so q v is odd. Since q is odd, v must be odd. So v is odd. The factor (p q + 2 p + 2 q) is integer. Let's compute modulo 2: p,q odd => p q odd. 2p and 2q are even. So p q + even + even = odd + even + even = odd. So the factor is odd. Thus, S(N) = odd * odd = odd. So S(N) is odd, hence not a multiple of 2, so cannot be a multiple of 6. So case B yields S(N) odd, not multiple of 6.

Thus, for a = 1, S(N) is either even and not divisible by 3 (case A) or odd (case B). In both cases, S(N) is not a multiple of 6. Therefore, we have proven:

**Lemma**: If N ≥ 2 has at least three proper divisors, then 6 ∤ N ⇒ 6 ∤ S(N).

Proof: Cases as above. We should also note that when N is even and not a multiple of 3, we have a ≥ 1. We covered a ≥ 2 and a = 1. For a ≥ 2, we proved S(N) not divisible by 6. For a = 1, we analyzed subcases and concluded S(N) not a multiple of 6. The argument for a = 1 required that we correctly identify d4. We need to ensure that in the case where m has at least two distinct odd primes, the third smallest divisor is indeed the second smallest odd prime q, provided that q < 2p. But is it possible that 2p < q and also that p^2 < q? Actually we already considered d4 = min(2p, q). So if q < 2p, then d4 = q. If q > 2p, then d4 = 2p. So the classification is correct. However, we also need to consider the possibility that m has a factor p^2 and no other odd primes, then d4 = 2p. That's covered in case A. If m has factor p^2 and also another odd prime q, then both p^2 and q are divisors. But p^2 is > 2p (since p>2), and q may be less than 2p. So the third smallest is min(2p, q). So still either 2p or q. So our cases cover all possibilities.

Now, also need to consider the scenario where m has only one odd prime factor p, but the exponent is ≥ 2, so m = p^k. Then the divisors of N are: 2, p, p^2, ..., p^k, and also 2p, 2p^2, etc. The smallest divisors >1 are 2, p, then what's the third? 2p is a divisor. Is it smaller than p^2? For p≥3, 2p < p^2 (since p^2-2p = p(p-2) > 0). So the third smallest is 2p. So d4 = 2p. That's case A. So indeed, case A covers all situations where the third smallest is 2p.

Now, for a = 1, we also need to consider the possibility that m is a prime power, and the exponent is 1 (i.e., m is prime). Then m = p (prime). Then N = 2p. The divisors are 1,2,p,2p. Proper divisors are 1,2,p. So there are three proper divisors. The three largest proper divisors are p,2,1. That's S(N) = p+2+1 = p+3. So S(N) = p+3. Since p is odd, p+3 is even. So S(N) is even. Also, we need to check if S(N) is divisible by 3. For p=5, S=8; p=11, S=14; p=17, S=20; p=23, S=26; p=29, S=32; etc. None are divisible by 3. But is there a prime p such that p+3 is divisible by 3? That would require p ≡ 0 mod 3, but p is prime and p ≠ 3, so p ≡ 1 or 2 mod 3. Then p+3 ≡ p ≡ 1 or 2 mod 3, not 0. So S(N) is not divisible by 3. So indeed, case A (with t = m/p = 1) yields S(N) = t(p+3) = p+3, even and not divisible by 3. So not a multiple of 6. So consistent.

Now, for a = 1 and m has at least two distinct odd primes, we need to check that in case B, d4 = q (second smallest odd prime) and that q < 2p, but also that 2p is a divisor, and we have d4 = min(2p, q). Since q < 2p, d4 = q. Then we computed S(N) = v * (p q + 2p + 2q). We need to also verify that v (which is m/(p q)) is odd. That's true because m is odd, p and q are odd, so m/(p q) is odd. So S(N) is odd. So not a multiple of 6.

Thus, the lemma is proven.

Now, we can use this lemma to show that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence cannot be all with at least 3 proper divisors. Because we can prove by induction that all a_n are not multiples of 6. But we need to be careful: The lemma says that if N has at least 3 proper divisors and 6 ∤ N, then S(N) is not a multiple of 6. So if we assume that a_n has at least 3 proper divisors and is not a multiple of 6, then a_{n+1} = S(a_n) is not a multiple of 6. So by induction, if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then all a_n are not multiples of 6. Also, we need to ensure that the sequence is defined (i.e., each a_n has at least 3 proper divisors). But we are trying to prove that such a1 cannot exist because it would lead to a contradiction (since eventually terms become ≤ 6 and cannot have ≥3 proper divisors). However, we need to also argue that the sequence is strictly decreasing. Is it guaranteed that if N is not a multiple of 6, then S(N) < N? Yes, we can prove that:

- If N is odd, then S(N) is odd, but is it < N? We need to examine. For odd N, we have S(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4. Since d2 ≥ 3 (odd), and N/d2 ≤ N/3, N/d3 ≤ N/5, etc. So S(N) ≤ N/3 + N/5 + N/7? Actually we need a bound. But we can prove that for any N with at least 3 proper divisors, S(N) ≤ N - 1? Not always. For N=6, S=6. For N=8, S=7 < 8. For N=10, S=8 < 10. For N=12, S=13 > 12. So it's not always less. But for N not a multiple of 6, we suspect S(N) < N. Let's test with N=15 (odd, not multiple of 6). S=9 < 15. N=21, S=11 < 21. N=33, S=15 < 33. N=35, S=13 < 35. N=39, S=17 < 39. N=45, S=29 < 45. N=55, S= ? 55=5*11, proper divisors 1,5,11 → sum 17, <55. So seems true. But we need a proof. We can use the same case analysis as before, but now we want to prove that if 6 ∤ N and N has at least 3 proper divisors, then S(N) < N. Actually we already have a lemma that S(N) is not a multiple of 6. That doesn't directly imply S(N) < N. However, we can combine with the fact that for odd N, S(N) is odd and we can bound S(N) ≤ N/3 + N/5 + N/7? That's not a strict bound. But maybe we can prove that S(N) < N for all N that are not multiples of 6. Let's attempt.

Take N odd. Then d2 ≥ 3. Then S(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4 ≤ N/3 + N/5 + N/7 (since d2,d3,d4 are at least 3,5,7). But this sum is less than N? For N=3, it's not defined, but N≥? For N=15, sum ≈ 5 + 3 + 2.14 ≈ 10.14 < 15. So seems plausible. However, we need a rigorous inequality: Since d2,d3,d4 are distinct integers >1, and N is odd, they are odd. The smallest possible triple is (3,5,7). So S(N) ≤ N/3 + N/5 + N/7. This is less than N for N≥? Actually compute N/3 + N/5 + N/7 = N * (1/3+1/5+1/7) = N * (35/105 + 21/105 + 15/105) = N * 71/105 ≈ 0.676N. So S(N) ≤ 0.676N, which is < N for all N > 0. So S(N) < N. Good. But is it possible that d2,d3,d4 are not (3,5,7) but something like (3,5,9) which would give sum N/3+N/5+N/9 = N*(1/3+1/5+1/9) = N*(15/45+9/45+5/45) = N*29/45 ≈ 0.644N, also < N. So indeed, S(N) ≤ N * (1/3+1/5+1/7) is an upper bound? Actually we need to bound from above: For N odd, the three smallest divisors >1 are at least 3,5,7. But they could be larger. So S(N) = N/d2+N/d3+N/d4 ≤ N/3+N/5+N/7. Since d2 ≥ 3, N/d2 ≤ N/3. But careful: If d2 = 3, then N/3 is integer. If d2 > 3, then N/d2 is smaller, so the sum is even smaller. So indeed, S(N) ≤ N/3+N/5+N/7. That's a valid inequality. So S(N) ≤ (71/105)N < N. So S(N) < N.

Now, take N even but not a multiple of 3. We'll need to prove S(N) < N. For N even, we already have a case analysis based on a (exponent of 2). We can bound S(N) similarly.

- If a ≥ 2, we have S(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/d4. Since N is divisible by 4. Then S(N) ≤ N/2+N/4+N/8 (if a≥3, d4=8) or N/2+N/4+N/p (if a=2). But we can bound S(N) ≤ N/2+N/4+N/8 = 7N/8 < N. For a=2, we have d4 = p (odd prime ≥5). Then N/d4 = N/p. Since N = 4m, and p divides m, N/p = 4m/p = 4t, with t = m/p. Also, N/2 = 2m, N/4 = m. So S(N) = 2m + m + 4t = 3m + 4t. Since m = p t, S = 3p t + 4t = t(3p+4). As argued, t is odd, 3p+4 is odd, so S is odd. Also, S ≤ ? To bound, we can note that p ≥ 5, so 3p+4 ≥ 19, and t = m/p ≤ m/5? But we need an inequality that S < N. Since N = 4 m, and S = t(3p+4). We can try to prove S ≤ (3p+4)/4 * m? Actually N = 4 m. So we need to show S < N. Compare S and N. S = t(3p+4). Since m = p t, N = 4 p t. So S/N = t(3p+4) / (4 p t) = (3p+4) / (4p) = 3/4 + 1/p. For p≥5, this is ≤ 3/4 + 1/5 = 0.75 + 0.2 = 0.95. So S ≤ 0.95 N < N. That's a valid bound, but we need to ensure that S is integer and that S < N. Since (3p+4)/(4p) < 1 for p>0. So S ≤ floor? Actually S = t(3p+4). N = 4 p t. So S/N = (3p+4)/(4p). Since S and N are integers, we can multiply. For any t, S/N = (3p+4)/(4p). This is less than 1. So S < N. That's a direct comparison without bounding by the sum of reciprocals. Indeed, S = t(3p+4). N = 4 p t. Since (3p+4) < 4p for p≥? Compute 4p - (3p+4) = p-4. For p≥5, p-4 ≥1, so 3p+4 ≤ 4p-1 < 4p. Hence, S < N. So for a=2, S < N. For a≥3, S = 7N/8 < N. So indeed, S(N) < N for all N even not multiple of 3.

Thus, we have:

- If N is odd (and has ≥3 proper divisors), S(N) < N.
- If N is even and not a multiple of 3, S(N) < N.

Now, the lemma we proved earlier says that if N is not a multiple of 6, then S(N) is not a multiple of 6. So we can use these to argue:

Assume a1 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can prove by induction that for all n, a_n is not a multiple of 6. Because if a_n is not a multiple of 6, then by the lemma, a_{n+1} is not a multiple of 6. Also, we need to ensure that the sequence is defined (a_n has ≥3 proper divisors). But we are proving that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then it's impossible for the sequence to be defined for all n with each a_n having ≥3 proper divisors. So we assume, for contradiction, that such a sequence exists (all a_n have ≥3 proper divisors). Then a1 is not a multiple of 6. Then a2 = S(a1) is not a multiple of 6 (by lemma). Similarly, a3 is not a multiple of 6, etc. So all a_n are not multiples of 6. Also, from the bound, S(N) < N for N not a multiple of 6, so the sequence is strictly decreasing. Since it's strictly decreasing and all terms are positive integers, it must eventually become ≤ 6. But we also have that all a_n are not multiples of 6. Therefore, eventually a_n ≤ 6 and a_n ≠ 6. The only possible values ≤ 6 that are not multiples of 6 are 1,2,3,4,5. All of those have fewer than 3 proper divisors. But a_n must have at least 3 proper divisors (by definition of the sequence). Contradiction. Hence, a1 must be a multiple of 6.

Thus, necessity is proven.

Now, we need to determine all possible a1 that are multiples of 6. But not all multiples of 6 work. So we need to find the subset of multiples of 6 for which the sequence is good. We'll do that by analyzing the orbit for a1 that is a multiple of 6.

Let a1 = N, 6 | N. Then define a2 = S(N). We want to know whether the sequence can consist of numbers each with at least 3 proper divisors. We'll need to examine the possible behavior.

We can try to prove that if N is a multiple of 6, then the sequence is good if and only if either (i) v2(N) = 1 and 5 ∤ N, or (ii) v2(N) ≥ 3 and N/72 is odd and 5 ∤ N/72. But we need to also consider the possibility that N is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 4, i.e., v2(N)=1. Those are in case (i). If v2(N)≥2, then N is divisible by 4, so N is a multiple of 12. Then we can consider subcases. But we also need to consider the possibility that N is a multiple of 6 and v2(N)≥2 but N is not a multiple of 72. Then a2 = 13N/12 (since triple (2,3,4)). But we need to check if a2 has at least 3 proper divisors. If a2 is prime or prime square, then it fails. But we can analyze the orbit: a2 will be odd (if N/12 is odd) or even (if N/12 is even). Let's test.

If N is a multiple of 12 but not of 72, then N/12 is not a multiple of 6. In fact, N/12 is an integer. Then a2 = 13 * (N/12). Since N/12 is integer, a2 is integer. We need to check whether a2 is a multiple of 6. If N/12 is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. But could a2 have at least 3 proper divisors? It might. However, we can try to prove that if N is a multiple of 6 and not in the two types, then the sequence will eventually encounter a term with <3 proper divisors. We can do this by induction or by analyzing the forward orbit.

But we already have a contradiction argument: If a1 is not a multiple of 6, then it's impossible. So a1 must be a multiple of 6. Now, we need to find all multiples of 6 for which the sequence is good. That is, we need to characterize the set G.

We can attempt to prove:

- If N is a multiple of 6 and also N is a fixed point (i.e., S(N)=N) and N has at least 3 proper divisors, then obviously the sequence is constant, good.
- If N is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point, then we need to examine whether the orbit stays good.

We can also try to prove that the only way for the sequence to be infinite with all terms having ≥3 proper divisors is if a1 is a multiple of 6 and the sequence becomes constant after at most 2 steps. That's a kind of "eventually constant" condition. So we can try to prove that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and the sequence is good, then a2 is a fixed point (or a1 itself is a fixed point). And then we can classify.

But we saw that a1=72 is not a fixed point, but a2=78 is fixed, so indeed the sequence becomes constant after one step. So that fits.

Now, we need to prove that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and the sequence is good, then a2 must be a fixed point. However, is it possible that a1 is a multiple of 6, a2 is not a fixed point, but a3 is a fixed point, and the sequence is good? That would be a longer chain. For example, a1=12, a2=13 (prime), not good. a1=36, a2=39 (composite), a3=25 (prime square), fails. a1=48, a2=52, a3=91, a4=21, a5=11, fails. a1=60, a2=65, a3=19, fails. a1=84, a2=91, a3=21, a4=11, fails. a1=96, a2=104, a3=91, fails. a1=108, a2=117, a3=61, fails. a1=120, a2=130, a3=104, fails. We can attempt to construct. Suppose N is multiple of 6. Then a2 = 13N/12 if N is multiple of 12. For a2 to be a fixed point, we need that a2 is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5. So if a2 is not fixed, it's either 31N/30. Then we can compute a3 = S(a2). That might be something else. So it's plausible that the only good a1 are those that map to a fixed point in one step. But we need a proof.

We can do a case analysis based on the exponent of 2 in a1. Since a1 is a multiple of 6. Write a1 = 2^a * 3^b * c, with c odd and not divisible by 2 or 3. Since a1 is multiple of 6, we have a ≥ 1, b ≥ 1. Then:

- If a = 1 and b ≥ 1. Then a1 is divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4. So a1 = 2 * 3 * m, with m odd, and 3 ∤ m? Actually we can factor out 3^b. Let's keep as a1 = 2 * 3 * k, where k is odd and may contain powers of 3? Actually if b≥2, then a1 has factor 3^2 = 9. But then a1 is divisible by 2 and 9, but not by 4. That's allowed. The triple (d2,d3,d4) depends on whether a1 is divisible by 4 (it's not) and whether it's divisible by 5. So we have:

   * If 5 ∤ a1, then d2=2, d3=3 (since 3 divides a1), d4=6 (since 6 divides a1). Then S(a1)=a1 (fixed point). So such a1 are good.
   * If 5 ∣ a1, then d2=2, d3=3, d4=5 (since 5 < 6). Then S(a1)=a1/2+a1/3+a1/5 = (31/30)a1. Since a1 is a multiple of 30 (because it's multiple of 6 and 5), we have a2 = 31 * (a1/30). Then a2 is odd. We need to check whether a2 has at least 3 proper divisors. Possibly it does, but then the sequence will decrease. We can analyze a2's image. We'll need to prove that such a1 are not good. That is, if a1 is a multiple of 6 and also a multiple of 5, then the sequence fails. We'll need to prove that.

- If a ≥ 2. Then a1 is divisible by 4. Since a1 is multiple of 6, it's divisible by 12. So a1 = 12 * t, where t is integer. Then we compute S(a1) = a1/2 + a1/3 + a1/4 = 13N/12 = 13t. So a2 = 13t. Now, we need to determine whether the sequence is good. That depends on t. If t is a multiple of 6, then a2 is a multiple of 6. Then we need to see if a2 is a fixed point or not. But if t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then the sequence will eventually fail because it will strictly decrease from a2 onward. So such a1 are not good. Thus, a1 must be such that t is a multiple of 6. That is, a1 is divisible by 72. Then a2 = 13 * (a1/12) = 13 * (6 * (a1/72)) = 78 * (a1/72). So a2 = 78 * u, where u = a1/72. Now, a2 is a multiple of 6. For the sequence to be good, we need that a2 is a fixed point, i.e., v2(a2)=1 and 5 ∤ a2. As computed, v2(a2) = 1 + v2(u). So we need v2(u)=0, i.e., u odd. Also, 5 ∤ a2 iff 5 ∤ u. So we need u odd and not divisible by 5. That's the condition.

Thus, the good a1 are those with:

- a1 ≡ 0 mod 6.
- Either (i) a1 is not divisible by 4 (i.e., v2(a1)=1) and a1 is not divisible by 5, or
- (ii) a1 is divisible by 72 and a1/72 is odd and not divisible by 5.

But note: In case (i), a1 is not divisible by 4, so v2(a1)=1. Also, a1 is not divisible by 5. However, is it possible that a1 is not divisible by 4 and also not divisible by 5, but is divisible by 3 and 2, so it's a multiple of 6. That's exactly the fixed points. But we also need to consider that a1 might have v2(a1)=1 and also be divisible by 5? That's not good. So the condition is necessary and sufficient.

Now, we also need to verify that if a1 satisfies (i), then a1 itself is a fixed point, so the sequence is constant. That's good. If a1 satisfies (ii), then a2 is a fixed point, so after one step it becomes constant. So those are good.

But we also need to check that a1 itself has at least 3 proper divisors. In (i), a1 is a multiple of 6, so it has at least 4 divisors, so condition holds. In (ii), a1 is a multiple of 72, so also has many divisors. So all good.

Now, are there any a1 that are multiples of 6 but not in (i) or (ii) that still produce an infinite good sequence? For example, a1 = 6 * something that is not a multiple of 4, but also not a multiple of 5. That's (i). For a1 = 72 * something that is even, then a2 is not a fixed point, but a2 might be such that its image a3 becomes fixed and all terms have ≥3 proper divisors. Let's examine a1 = 144 (which is 72*2). We saw a2=156, a3=169, fails. a1 = 216 (72*3) works. a1 = 360 (72*5) fails. So indeed only those with odd u and not divisible by 5 work. But could there be a1 that is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 72, yet a2 is not a multiple of 6, but a2 has at least 3 proper divisors and then a3 becomes fixed (maybe) without hitting a bad number? We need to check.

Take a1 = 12 (multiple of 6, not multiple of 72). a2 = 13 (prime). Fails. a1 = 36 → a2 = 39 (composite, but a3=25 fails). a1 = 48 → a2 = 52, a3 = 91, a4=21 fails. a1 = 60 → a2 = 65, a3=19 fails. a1 = 84 → a2=91, a3=21 fails. a1 = 96 → a2=104, a3=91, fails. So seems they all fail. But we need a proof. We can argue: If a1 is a multiple of 6 and v2(a1) ≥ 2, then a1 is divisible by 12. Write a1 = 12 * t. Then a2 = 13t. Now, if t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can apply the decreasing argument to a2: Since a2 is not a multiple of 6 (and we assume that a2 has at least 3 proper divisors, as it's part of the sequence), then by the lemma, a3 = S(a2) is not a multiple of 6 and is smaller than a2. So the sequence will strictly decrease from a2 onward. Eventually, it will hit a term ≤ 6 that is not a multiple of 6, which cannot have ≥3 proper divisors. Thus, such a1 cannot be good. So indeed, for a1 to be good, we must have t multiple of 6. That is, a1 divisible by 72. So that's necessary.

Similarly, if a1 is not divisible by 4, then a1 = 2 * 3 * m with m odd, and we have two subcases: if 5 ∤ a1, then a2 = a1 (fixed point), good; if 5 ∣ a1, then a2 = 31 * (a1/30). Now, a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can apply the decreasing argument to a2. Since a2 is not a multiple of 6, and we assume that a2 has at least 3 proper divisors (as it's part of the sequence), then by the lemma, a3 is not a multiple of 6 and smaller. So the sequence will strictly decrease from a2 onward. Eventually, it will hit a term ≤ 6 that is not a multiple of 6, contradiction. Thus, such a1 cannot be good. Therefore, if a1 is a multiple of 6 and not in (i) or (ii), then the sequence fails. So the only possible a1 are those in (i) or (ii).

Thus, the answer set is:

- All numbers that are multiples of 6, are not divisible by 4, and are not divisible by 5.
- All numbers that are multiples of 72, and such that the quotient (a1/72) is odd and not divisible by 5.

We can also express this more compactly:

Let a1 be a positive integer. Then the infinite sequence exists if and only if either

   (1) a1 is a multiple of 6, not a multiple of 4, and not a multiple of 5; or
   (2) a1 is a multiple of 72, and a1/72 is odd and not a multiple of 5.

But we should also consider the possibility that a1 is a multiple of 6 and also is a multiple of 4, but a1/72 is even, yet maybe a2 is a multiple of 6 and not fixed, but a3 becomes fixed and all terms have ≥3 proper divisors. We argued that if a1 is divisible by 12 but not by 72, then a2 is not a multiple of 6, so decreasing argument applies. But could it be that a2 is a multiple of 6 (if t is multiple of 6) but a1 is not divisible by 72? Wait if a1 is divisible by 12, then t = a1/12. If t is a multiple of 6, then a1/12 is multiple of 6 => a1 is multiple of 72. So indeed, if a1 is divisible by 12 and t is multiple of 6, then a1 is divisible by 72. So that's covered. So any a1 that is a multiple of 12 but not a multiple of 72 leads to a2 not a multiple of 6. So fails.

Now, we should also consider a1 that is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 12, i.e., v2(a1)=1. That's the case where a1 is even but not divisible by 4. Then a2 is either a1 (if 5 ∤ a1) or 31*(a1/30). In the latter case, a2 is not a multiple of 6. So fails. So only those with 5 ∤ a1 are good. So that's (i). But is it possible that a1 is a multiple of 6 and v2(a1)=1, and also a1 is a multiple of 5, but then a2 is not a multiple of 6, and then a3 becomes a fixed point? For that to happen, we would need that a2 is such that its image a3 is a fixed point and all intermediate terms have ≥3 proper divisors. But a2 is not a multiple of 6, so by the lemma, a3 is not a multiple of 6 and is smaller. So it's decreasing. It cannot become a fixed point (which is a multiple of 6) later. So it will strictly decrease. So fails. So indeed, those are not good.

Thus, the classification is correct.

Now, we need to present the solution in a clear, rigorous way. Let's outline the solution:

1. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N, assuming N has at least three proper divisors.
2. The sequence a_n is defined by a_1 = N, a_{n+1} = f(a_n). The condition says each a_n has at least three proper divisors, so f is defined for all n.
3. Goal: Determine all possible N (a1) for which such a sequence exists.

We'll prove:

**Lemma 1**: For any integer N ≥ 2 with at least three proper divisors,
   - If N is odd, then f(N) is odd.
   - If N is even and not a multiple of 3, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6 (i.e., it is not both even and divisible by 3). Actually we need a lemma that f(N) is not a multiple of 6 when N is not a multiple of 6. We'll prove that using case analysis.

But we already have a combined lemma that if N has at least three proper divisors and 6 ∤ N, then 6 ∤ f(N). Let's prove that.

We can break into cases:

- N odd → f(N) odd → not multiple of 6.
- N even, 3 ∤ N. Write N = 2^a * m, with m odd, a ≥ 1. Since 3 ∤ N, m not divisible by 3. Then consider a:
   * If a ≥ 2: N divisible by 4. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2, 4, and either 8 (if a≥3) or the smallest odd divisor p (if a=2). In both subcases, we can compute f(N) and show it's not a multiple of 6. We can present a unified argument: For a ≥ 2, f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/d, where d is the third smallest divisor. Then we can show that f(N) is not divisible by 3 (by evaluating modulo 3) and also that f(N) is not necessarily even but we need to show that 6 ∤ f(N). Actually if f(N) is odd, then it's not multiple of 6. If it's even, we need to show it's not divisible by 3. So we can compute f(N) modulo 3. But careful: For a ≥ 2, N is divisible by 4, so N is divisible by 2^2. In the case a=2, we have N = 4m. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/p = 2m + m + (4m)/p = 3m + 4t (where t = m/p). As we argued, t is odd (since m is odd, p is odd). So f(N) is odd. So not multiple of 6. For a ≥ 3, f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/8 = 7N/8. Then N is divisible by 8, so N is divisible by 2^3. Then f(N) is integer. We can check mod 3: N ≡ ±1 → f(N) ≡ 2N + N + 2N = 5N ≡ 2N (mod 3), not 0. So f(N) is not divisible by 3. Also, f(N) may be even or odd. But since it's not divisible by 3, it cannot be a multiple of 6. So indeed, for a ≥ 2, f(N) is not a multiple of 6.

   * If a = 1: N is even but not divisible by 4. Write N = 2m, m odd. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/d4, where p is the smallest odd prime factor of m, and d4 is the third smallest divisor. We need to analyze subcases. We already did that: d4 = 2p or q (second odd prime). In either case, we can compute f(N) and show it's not a multiple of 6. In the case d4 = 2p, f(N) = m + 2t + t = m + 3t = t(p+3) with t = m/p (odd). Then f(N) is even but not divisible by 3 (since p t ≠ 0 mod 3). In the case d4 = q, f(N) = v (p q + 2p + 2q) with v odd, and the factor (p q + 2p + 2q) is odd, so f(N) is odd. So f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Good.

Thus, the lemma is proven.

Now, with this lemma, we can show necessity: If a1 is not a multiple of 6, then by induction all a_n are not multiples of 6, and also S(N) < N for N not a multiple of 6, so the sequence is strictly decreasing. Then eventually a_n ≤ 6 and a_n ≠ 6, contradiction. So a1 must be a multiple of 6.

But is the "S(N) < N" part always true for N not a multiple of 6? We argued that for odd N, S(N) ≤ (71/105)N < N. For even N not multiple of 3, we argued that S(N) < N via direct comparison. Let's confirm for even N not multiple of 3 with a=1 (N even not divisible by 4). In that case, S(N) = t(p+3) with t odd, and N = 2 p t. Since p+3 < 2p for p≥5? Actually compare (p+3) and 2p. For p=5, p+3=8, 2p=10 → 8<10. For p=11, 14<22. For p=17, 20<34. So (p+3) < 2p, thus S(N) = t(p+3) < t * 2p = N. So S(N) < N. For a=2, S(N) = t(3p+4) with N=4p t. Compare (3p+4) and 4p: 4p - (3p+4) = p-4 ≥ 1 for p≥5. So S(N) < N. For a≥3, S(N)=7N/8 < N. So indeed, S(N) < N for all N not multiple of 6 (with ≥3 proper divisors). Good.

Thus, the decreasing argument works.

Now, for a1 that are multiples of 6, we need to determine which ones are good. We'll do a case analysis based on the exponent of 2 in a1.

Let N = a1. Since 6|N, N = 2^a * 3^b * c, with c odd and not divisible by 2 or 3. a ≥ 1, b ≥ 1.

We'll consider two main cases: a = 1 and a ≥ 2.

Case a = 1: N is even but not divisible by 4. Then N = 2 * 3^b * c, with c odd, not divisible by 2 or 3. Then:

- If 5 ∤ N, then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2, 3, 6. Then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N. So a2 = N, constant, good.
- If 5 ∣ N, then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = (31/30)N. Since N is a multiple of 30, write N = 30 * s. Then a2 = 31 * s. Now, a2 is odd. Also, a2 has at least three proper divisors? It might or might not. But we need to show that such N cannot be good. Because we already have a contradiction argument: If a1 is good, then all terms are good. For a1 with a=1 and 5|N, we can show that a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then by the lemma, a3 is not a multiple of 6 and a3 < a2. So the sequence strictly decreases from a2 onward. Eventually it will encounter a term ≤ 6 that is not 6, contradiction. So such N are not good.

But is it possible that a2 is a multiple of 6? Let's compute a2 = 31 * s. For a2 to be a multiple of 6, we need s to be a multiple of 6 (since 31 is not). But s = N/30. Since N is multiple of 6, N/30 might be integer. Could s be a multiple of 6? That would require N to be a multiple of 180. But then a2 = 31 * (multiple of 6) is a multiple of 6? 31 * (6k) = 186k, which is divisible by 6. So a2 could be a multiple of 6 if s is a multiple of 6. However, does such N exist? For N to be a multiple of 6 and also a multiple of 5, N must be a multiple of 30. If N is a multiple of 30 and also a multiple of 6 (which is automatic), and we consider a=1 case (N not divisible by 4), can N be a multiple of 30 and also not divisible by 4? Yes, e.g., N = 30 itself, a=1? 30 is divisible by 2, but not by 4, so a=1. N=30 is not a multiple of 4, so a=1. N=30, s = 1. a2 = 31. Not multiple of 6. N=90, s=3. a2=93, not multiple. N=150, s=5. a2=155. Not multiple. N=210, s=7. a2=217. Not multiple. N=270, s=9. a2=279. Not multiple. N=330, s=11. a2=341. Not multiple. So s is odd, not multiple of 6. Could there be N with a=1 and 5|N such that s is a multiple of 6? That would require N to be a multiple of 180. But then N is divisible by 180 = 2^2 * 3^2 * 5. Since 180 has 2^2, N would be divisible by 4. That contradicts a=1. So indeed, if a=1, then N is not divisible by 4, so it cannot be divisible by 4, thus cannot be divisible by 180 (which has factor 4). So s cannot be a multiple of 6. So a2 is not a multiple of 6. Good. So our argument holds.

Thus, in case a=1, the only good N are those with 5 ∤ N. (We also need to consider that N must have at least 3 proper divisors, which holds because N is a multiple of 6.)

Now, case a ≥ 2: N is divisible by 4. Since N is a multiple of 6, it is divisible by 12. So N = 12 * t, t integer. Then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13t. So a2 = 13t.

Now, if t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can apply the decreasing argument to a2 (since a2 has at least 3 proper divisors as part of the sequence). But we need to be careful: We are trying to determine whether N is good. If t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then by the lemma, a3 = S(a2) is not a multiple of 6 and is smaller. So the sequence will strictly decrease from a2 onward, eventually hitting a term ≤ 6 that is not 6, contradiction. Therefore, such N cannot be good. So for N to be good, we must have t a multiple of 6, i.e., N divisible by 72.

Thus, a ≥ 2 and N divisible by 72. Then a2 = 13 * (N/12) = 13 * (6 * (N/72)) = 78 * u, where u = N/72. So a2 = 78 u.

Now, we need to check whether the sequence is good. a2 is a multiple of 6, so we need to examine its behavior. If a2 is a fixed point, then the sequence becomes constant. a2 is a fixed point if and only if v2(a2) = 1 and 5 ∤ a2. Compute v2(a2) = v2(78 u) = v2(78) + v2(u) = 1 + v2(u). So v2(a2) = 1 iff v2(u) = 0, i.e., u is odd. Also, a2 is not divisible by 5 iff 5 ∤ u. Therefore, if u is odd and not divisible by 5, then a2 is a fixed point, and the sequence is good. If u is even or divisible by 5, then a2 is not a fixed point. But can the sequence still be good? We need to analyze further. Let's consider subcases:

- If u is even, then a2 has v2 ≥ 2, so it is not a fixed point. Moreover, a2 is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point. Then we can examine its image a3 = S(a2). Since a2 is a multiple of 6, we can apply the same analysis to a2. But note that a2 may be such that its image is a fixed point. However, we need to check if the sequence can be all good. We can try to prove that if a2 is not a fixed point, then the sequence will eventually encounter a term with <3 proper divisors. But maybe we can argue by induction: If a1 is good, then a2 must be a fixed point. But is that necessarily true? Let's test with a1 = 72 (u=1 odd, not divisible by 5) works. a1 = 216 (u=3 odd, not divisible by 5) works. a1 = 360 (u=5, odd but divisible by 5) fails. a1 = 144 (u=2 even) fails. So indeed, for a1 good, we need a2 to be a fixed point. So we need to show that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and is good, then a2 is a fixed point. But is it possible that a1 is a multiple of 6 and a2 is not a fixed point, yet the sequence is still good? That would require that a2 is not a fixed point, but a3 is a fixed point, and all terms have ≥3 proper divisors. Let's try to see if we can find such a chain with a1 multiple of 6. Suppose a1 = 72 * u, with u even. Then a2 = 78 u. Since u is even, a2 has v2 ≥ 2. Then a2 is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point. Now, a2 = 78 u. Write u = 2^k * w with w odd. Then a2 = 78 * 2^k * w. v2(a2) = 1 + k. So if k≥1, v2(a2) ≥ 2. Then a2 is not a fixed point. Now, compute a3 = S(a2). We can try to see if a3 could be a fixed point. Let's test with a1=144 (u=2), a2=156, a3=169 (prime square). That fails. a1=216 (u=3) works. a1=360 (u=5) fails. a1=504 (u=7) works. So it seems that when u is even, a2 is not a fixed point and the sequence fails. We need a proof. The proof could be: If a1 is good, then a2 = S(a1) is also good. But we can also analyze the orbit: Since a2 is not a fixed point, we can try to apply the decreasing argument to a2? But a2 is a multiple of 6, so the decreasing argument (S(N) < N) does not necessarily hold for multiples of 6. In fact, S(N) could be greater than N. So we cannot directly say that a3 < a2. But perhaps we can show that if a2 is not a fixed point, then a2 is either odd (if it's not multiple of 6) or if it's multiple of 6 but not fixed, then its image a3 will be smaller than a2? Let's check with a2=156 (which is a multiple of 6, but not fixed). S(156) = 169 > 156. So it increased. That's not smaller. So we can't rely on decreasing.

But we have a different approach: We already proved necessity that a1 must be a multiple of 6. Then we are trying to determine which multiples of 6 are good. We can do a direct characterization: We can prove that if N is a multiple of 6, then the sequence is good iff either N is a fixed point (which is equivalent to N not divisible by 4 and 5 ∤ N) or N is a multiple of 72 and N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5. But we also need to consider the possibility that N is a multiple of 6, N is not a fixed point, but N/72 is odd and not divisible by 5, then a2 is fixed, so good. So that's covered. What about N that is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 72? Then a2 is not a multiple of 6, and we argued that it leads to failure. But we need to prove that it leads to failure. That's covered by the decreasing argument applied to a2, because a2 is not a multiple of 6. But we also need to ensure that a2 indeed has at least 3 proper divisors (as it is part of the sequence). If a1 is good, then all a_n are good, so a2 has at least 3 proper divisors. So we can assume that. Then we apply the lemma to a2: Since a2 is not a multiple of 6, then a3 is not a multiple of 6 and is smaller, etc. So strictly decreasing. Then eventually it will hit a term ≤ 6 that is not a multiple of 6, contradiction. Therefore, a1 cannot be good if t is not a multiple of 6. So that's sufficient.

Now, what about N that is a multiple of 6 and also a multiple of 72 but with u even? Then a2 = 78 u. Since u is even, a2 is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point. We need to analyze whether such a1 can be good. We can try to apply a similar argument to a2? But a2 is a multiple of 6, so we cannot directly say that a3 is smaller. However, we can try to show that a2 is not a fixed point and then look at its image a3. Maybe we can prove that if a2 is not a fixed point, then a3 will be such that the sequence fails. But is it possible that a2 is not a fixed point, but a3 is a fixed point and the sequence is good? Let's test with a2=156 (not fixed), a3=169 (prime square). That fails. With a2=78 * 2 = 156, fails. With a2=78 * 4 = 312, a3 = S(312) = 104? Actually 312 is 2^3 * 3 * 13. Compute S(312) = 312/2 + 312/4 + 312/6? Wait triple: d2=2, d3=3? But 3 divides 312, so d3=3, d4=4? Actually 312's divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,12,13,24,26,39,52,78,104,156,312. Smallest >1: 2,3,4 → triple (2,3,4). Then S=156+104+78=338. 338 is 2*13^2, which has proper divisors 1,2,13,26,169. That's 5 proper divisors. But is 338 a fixed point? v2(338)=1, 5 ∤ 338 → it's a fixed point. So a3=338 is a fixed point. Then the sequence becomes constant. So if a2=312, then a3=338 (fixed). So that would be a good chain if a1 leads to a2=312. Does such a1 exist? a2=312 corresponds to a1 = 12 * t with t = a2/13 = 24. So a1 = 12 * 24 = 288. a1=288 is a multiple of 6, and a1/72 = 4 (even). So a1=288. Let's test a1=288. Compute a2 = S(288). 288 = 2^5 * 3^2. Triple (2,3,4) gives S=13*24 = 312. So a2=312. a3 = S(312) = 13*24? Actually 312/12=26, S=13*26=338? Wait compute: 312 = 2^3 * 3 * 13. The triple is (2,3,4) -> S = 156+104+78 = 338. That's 338, which is a fixed point. So a3=338. Then the sequence becomes constant at 338. So a1=288 is good? But we earlier tested 288 and thought it fails. Let's test manually: a1=288 (divisible by 6, a=5). a2 = sum of three largest proper divisors of 288. Divisors of 288: 1,2,3,4,6,8,9,12,16,18,24,32,36,48,72,96,144,288. Largest three proper: 144,96,72? Actually 144 is largest proper, then 96, then 72. Sum = 144+96+72 = 312. So a2=312. a3 = sum of three largest proper divisors of 312. Divisors of 312: 1,2,3,4,6,8,12,13,24,26,39,52,78,104,156,312. Largest three proper: 156,104,78. Sum = 156+104+78 = 338. So a3=338. a4 = sum of three largest proper divisors of 338. 338 = 2 * 13^2. Divisors: 1,2,13,26,169,338. Largest three proper: 169,26,13. Sum = 169+26+13 = 208. So a4=208. 208 is 2^4 * 13. Proper divisors: 1,2,4,8,13,16,26,52,104. Largest three: 104,52,26 sum = 182. So a5=182. 182 = 2 * 7 * 13. Largest three: 91,26,14 sum = 131. a6=131 (prime). So indeed, the sequence fails at a6. But we earlier computed a3=338, which is a fixed point. But is 338 a fixed point? Let's verify: 338 = 2 * 13^2. The three largest proper divisors are 169,26,13 sum = 208, not 338. So 338 is not a fixed point. I made a mistake: I said that a3=338 is a fixed point because v2(338)=1 and 5 ∤ 338. But 338 is not a fixed point; the fixed point condition for a number N is S(N)=N. For 338, we computed S(338)=208. So it's not fixed. So my earlier claim that a3=338 is a fixed point is false. Indeed, we need to compute S(338) correctly. So a2=312, a3=338, a4=208, etc. So the sequence fails. So a1=288 is not good.

Thus, the classification is correct.

Now, we need to prove that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and also a multiple of 72, but with u even or divisible by 5, then the sequence is not good. That's the part where we need to show that the orbit eventually fails. The decreasing argument applied to a2 fails because a2 may be a multiple of 6. But we can try to apply the decreasing argument to a2 after some steps? Let's examine.

But does 7 have at least three proper divisors? 7 is prime, only proper divisor is 1, so it fails. So a1=8 leads to a2=7, which violates condition. So 8 is not allowed.
