Let's explore the function f(N) more systematically. Let's denote N's prime factorization. We need to compute the three largest proper divisors. Equivalent to: The three largest proper divisors are N divided by the three smallest divisors > 1 (including 1?). Actually the largest proper divisor is N / p_min. The second largest proper divisor is N / d where d is the second smallest divisor > 1. But careful: If N has many divisors, the third largest proper divisor is N / d where d is the third smallest divisor > 1. However is it always true that the three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors > 1? Let's confirm with example: N=12, divisors ascending: 1,2,3,4,6,12. Smallest divisors >1: 2,3,4. Then N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3. Indeed those are the three largest proper divisors (6,4,3). So that holds. For N=18, divisors: 1,2,3,6,9,18. Smallest >1: 2,3,6. N/2=9, N/3=6, N/6=3. The three largest proper divisors are 9,6,3 (sum 18). So indeed they are N/p, N/q, N/r where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1. However note that if N is a square, then the divisor sqrt(N) appears, and it is the middle one. For N=36, divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,9,12,18,36. Smallest >1: 2,3,4. Then N/2=18, N/3=12, N/4=9. Those are the three largest proper divisors (18,12,9). That matches. So the mapping is correct. So we can compute f(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4 where d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1 (i.e., the smallest three proper divisors, but not including 1). However note that if N has only two proper divisors, then d4 would not exist. So we assume N has at least three proper divisors. So d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1.

Thus f(N) = N * (1/d2 + 1/d3 + 1/d4). Since N is composite, these are integers.

Now we need to analyze iteration. A common technique is to consider the smallest prime factor and see how f(N) relates to N. Perhaps we can find that if N is even and not divisible by 3? Actually 6 is even and divisible by 3. 18 is even and divisible by 3. 42 is even and divisible by 3? 42 is divisible by 3? 42/3=14, yes divisible by 3. 54 is divisible by 3? 54/3=18, yes divisible by 3. 66 is divisible by 3? 66/3=22, yes. 78 is divisible by 3? 78/3=26, yes. So all these fixed points are divisible by 6. But 12 is divisible by 6 but fails. So what distinguishes them? Possibly they are numbers where the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3, and something like 4 or 5 or 6 etc. Let's compute for each fixed point:

- N=6: divisors >1: 2,3,6 (since 6 is composite). The three smallest are 2,3,6. Then f(6)=6/2+6/3+6/6 = 3+2+1 = 6.
- N=18: divisors >1: 2,3,6,9,18. Smallest three: 2,3,6. Then f(18)=9+6+3 = 18.
- N=42: divisors >1: 2,3,6,7,14,21,42. Smallest three: 2,3,6. Then f(42)=21+14+7 = 42. Wait 42/2=21, 42/3=14, 42/6=7. Yes.
- N=54: divisors >1: 2,3,6,9,18,27,54. Smallest three: 2,3,6. Then f(54)=27+18+9 = 54.
- N=66: divisors >1: 2,3,6,11,22,33,66. Smallest three: 2,3,6 => 33+22+11 = 66.
- N=78: divisors >1: 2,3,6,13,26,39,78 => 39+26+13 = 78.

So indeed for these numbers, the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3, and 6. That is a pattern: N is divisible by 2 and 3, and also by 6 (which is automatic). But they also have a divisor 6, which is the product of 2 and 3. So N is a multiple of 6. However, many multiples of 6 also have 2,3,6 as smallest divisors >1. For example, N=12: divisors >1: 2,3,4,6,12. Smallest three: 2,3,4. So they are 2,3,4. That's why f(12)=6+4+3=13, not 12. So the difference is that in N=12, the divisor 6 is not among the three smallest because 4 is smaller than 6. So the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4. The sum of the three largest proper divisors (N/2=6, N/3=4, N/4=3) is 13, not 12. So for N to be a fixed point, we need N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N, i.e., N/2+N/3+N/6 = (3N/6+N/3? Actually N/2+N/3+N/6 = (3N+2N+N)/6 = 6N/6 = N. So indeed if the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6, then f(N)=N. But is it necessary that the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6? Could there be other triples that also sum to N? For example, if the three smallest are 2,3, something else, maybe N/(something) plus N/2+N/3 could equal N. But note that the three largest proper divisors are determined by the three smallest divisors >1. So if f(N)=N, then N = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4. Dividing by N (N>0), we get 1 = 1/d2 + 1/d3 + 1/d4. So we need three integers >1 (since d2,d3,d4 are divisors >1) whose reciprocals sum to 1. This is a classic diophantine equation. The only solutions in integers >1 are (2,3,6) up to permutation, because 1/2+1/3+1/6=1. Also (2,4,4) gives 1/2+1/4+1/4=1, but 4 appears twice, but d2,d3,d4 must be distinct? Not necessarily distinct; they are the three smallest divisors >1, but if N has multiple divisors with the same value? They are distinct as numbers (divisors are distinct). So we cannot have repeats like (2,4,4) because that would require that 4 appears as a divisor twice? Actually divisors are numbers; the list of divisors is a set, so they are distinct. So d2,d3,d4 are distinct divisors. So we need three distinct integers >1 whose reciprocals sum to 1. The only solution (up to permutation) is (2,3,6). Indeed, the only Egyptian fraction representation of 1 with three unit fractions with distinct denominators is 1/2+1/3+1/6=1. There are also representations with denominators like 2,4,4 (but not distinct) or 3,3,3 (1/3+1/3+1/3=1) but then they are not distinct; however the three smallest divisors >1 could be 3,3,? but they must be distinct because divisors are distinct numbers. So if N has divisors 3 and 3, that's impossible. So the only possibility is (2,3,6). Therefore, if f(N)=N, then the three smallest divisors >1 are exactly 2,3,6. Conversely, if the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6, then f(N)=N.

Thus fixed points are precisely those composite numbers N such that N is divisible by 2 and 3 and also 6 is a divisor, which is automatic if divisible by 2 and 3; but we need that 4 (or any other divisor) is not smaller than 6. More precisely, we need that the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6. That means N is even (has divisor 2), N is divisible by 3, and also N has no divisor d with 1 < d < 6 except 2 and 3. That is, N is not divisible by any integer between 4 and 5? Actually integers between 1 and 6 exclusive: 2,3,4,5. So N must not be divisible by 4 or 5. But if N is divisible by 2 and 3, it's divisible by 6. For N to not be divisible by 4, N must not have 2^2 as a factor; that is, the exponent of 2 in N is 1. Similarly, N must not be divisible by 5, because if it is divisible by 5 then 5 is a divisor >1, and 5 < 6, so then the three smallest divisors >1 would be 2,3,5? Actually if N is divisible by 5, then 5 is a divisor. Since N is divisible by 2 and 3, the set of divisors includes 2,3,5,6, etc. The three smallest >1 would be 2,3,5 (if 5 is smaller than 6). Then f(N) would be N/2+N/3+N/5, which is not equal to N. So to have the triple (2,3,6) as the three smallest, we need that N is not divisible by any integer >1 less than 6 except 2 and 3. So N must be not divisible by 4 or 5. Also not divisible by any other numbers like 1? That's it. So N must be of the form N = 2 * 3 * m, where m is an integer such that N has no divisor 4 or 5. But careful: N could be divisible by 4 but also have 2 as a divisor; if N has factor 4, then 4 is a divisor, so the three smallest would be 2,3,4 (or 2,4,? depending on whether 3 is a divisor). So N cannot have 2^2 as a factor, i.e., exponent of 2 is 1. Similarly, N cannot have 5 as a factor. Also, N could be divisible by 7? That's okay as long as 7 is >6, but if N is divisible by 7, then 7 is a divisor >6, but does it affect the three smallest? The three smallest are still 2,3,6 provided N is not divisible by 4 or 5. However, note that if N is divisible by 2 and 3, then 6 is a divisor. If N is also divisible by 7, then the divisors include 2,3,6,7,14,21, etc. The three smallest >1 are 2,3,6. That's fine. So N is allowed to have other prime factors beyond 2 and 3, but the exponent of 2 must be 1. Also, no factor 5. Also, what about factor 4? Already covered. What about factor 8? That would give divisor 4 (since 2^2), so exponent of 2 must be 1. So N must be of the form N = 2 * 3 * k, where k is an integer not divisible by 2 or 5? Actually k could be divisible by 2? If N = 2 * 3 * k, then if k is even, then N has factor 2^2, so exponent of 2 becomes 2, which violates. So k must be odd. Also, k must not be divisible by 5. Additionally, we need to ensure that N does not have any divisor d with 1<d<6 besides 2 and 3. But are there any other numbers less than 6 besides 2,3,4,5? 4 and 5 are the only ones. So if N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then the only divisors less than 6 are 2 and 3. But we also need to check whether N could be divisible by something like 1? That's trivial. So the condition for fixed points is: N is composite, N has at least three proper divisors, and N is divisible by 2 and 3, but not by 4 or 5. However, is it necessary that N is divisible by 2 and 3? If N is fixed point, then f(N)=N. Then we derived that 1 = 1/d2+1/d3+1/d4. Since d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1. The only solution is (2,3,6). That means d2=2,d3=3,d4=6. So indeed N must be divisible by 2 and 3 (and also by 6). So N must be a multiple of 6. Moreover, the three smallest divisors >1 are exactly 2,3,6. So N cannot have any divisor <6 other than 2 and 3, i.e., cannot be divisible by 4 or 5. Also cannot be divisible by any number like 1? That's it. But could N be divisible by 2 and 3 but also have a divisor like 2^2? That would give 4 as a divisor, which is <6, so then the three smallest would be 2,3,4 (since 4 < 6). That would make d4=4, not 6. So such N would not be a fixed point. So indeed fixed points are multiples of 6 that are not divisible by 4 or 5. Also they must have at least three proper divisors, which is automatically satisfied if they are multiples of 6 and not too small? For example, N=6 works, N=18 works, N=42 works, N=54 works, N=66 works, N=78 works. So they are multiples of 6 with exponent of 2 equal to 1 (i.e., 2 divides N but 4 does not) and 5 does not divide N. So N is of the form N = 2 * 3 * m, where m is odd and not divisible by 5. But also note: If N is divisible by 2 and 3, then N is divisible by 6. The condition that N is not divisible by 4 means the exponent of 2 is exactly 1. So N = 2 * 3 * m, with m odd. That's automatically satisfied if m is odd and not divisible by 2. But we also need that N is not divisible by 5. So m should not be divisible by 5. So the set of fixed points is: N = 6 * k, where k is a positive integer such that N is not divisible by 4 or 5. But N=6k is divisible by 2 and 3. For N to be not divisible by 4, we need that 6k is not divisible by 4. Since 6 = 2*3, the factor 2 appears once. So 6k is divisible by 4 iff k is even (because then the exponent of 2 becomes at least 2). So k must be odd. For N to be not divisible by 5, we need that 6k is not divisible by 5, i.e., k is not a multiple of 5. So fixed points are N = 6 * k, where k is odd and not divisible by 5. But also we must have N has at least three proper divisors. For N=6, k=1 (odd, not multiple of 5). For N=18, k=3 (odd, not multiple of 5). For N=42, k=7 (odd, not multiple of 5). For N=54, k=9 (odd, not multiple of 5). For N=66, k=11 (odd, not multiple of 5). For N=78, k=13 (odd, not multiple of 5). So indeed that's the pattern.

Now the problem asks to determine all possible values of a1 (the first term) such that the infinite sequence a1,a2,... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. That means that if we start with a1 and apply f repeatedly, all terms must have at least three proper divisors. So a1 must be such that the orbit under f never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. However, note that the condition also includes that each a_n itself must have at least three proper divisors, so it's a property of the sequence. So we need to find a1 for which the recursively defined sequence (with a_{n+1}=f(a_n)) is well-defined and satisfies the condition. That's what we'll analyze.

Now we have determined fixed points: N such that f(N)=N, which are numbers that are multiples of 6 with exponent of 2 = 1 and not divisible by 5. But are there other cycles or eventual behavior? For example, a1=72 leads to a2=78 which is fixed. So 72 is allowed. What about a1=84? 84 = 2^2 * 3 * 7. Let's compute f(84). Divisors: 84's divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,7,12,14,21,28,42,84. Smallest >1: 2,3,4? Actually 4 is a divisor, so d2=2,d3=3,d4=4. Then f(84) = 84/2+84/3+84/4 = 42+28+21 = 91. 91 is 7*13, proper divisors: 1,7,13. Sum of three largest proper? Actually only two proper divisors (1,7,13? Wait 91's divisors: 1,7,13,91. Proper: 1,7,13. That's three proper divisors! So 91 has three proper divisors (1,7,13). So a2=91, a3 = sum of three largest proper divisors of 91 = 13+7+1 = 21. 21's proper divisors: 1,3,7. Sum = 11 (prime). So 21 fails. So 84 fails.

What about a1=30? 30 -> 31 fails. So not allowed.

Now consider a1 that is a fixed point, obviously works. Also a1 that eventually reaches a fixed point without hitting a deficient number. But could there be a cycle of length >1 where all numbers have at least three proper divisors? Possibly, but we need to examine. The function f(N) is defined based on the three smallest divisors >1. Typically f(N) is less than N? Let's compare. For N composite, N/d2 is at most N/2. Sum of three such terms could be less than N or greater? For N=12, f(N)=13 > N. So f(N) can be larger than N. So the sequence might increase. For N=6, f(N)=6 (fixed). For N=18, f(N)=18. For N=42, 42. For N=54, 54. For N=66, 66. For N=78, 78. For N=84, 91 > 84. For N=90? 90 = 2*3^2*5. Smallest >1: 2,3,5? Actually 90's divisors: 1,2,3,5,6,9,10,15,18,30,45,90. Smallest >1: 2,3,5. Then f(90) = 45+30+18 = 93. 93 = 3*31. Proper: 1,3,31 => sum = 35 (prime? 35 is composite, but has proper divisors 1,5,7? Actually 35's divisors: 1,5,7,35. Proper: 1,5,7. So 35 has three proper divisors. Then a3 = f(35) = 7+5+1 = 13 (prime) fails. So 90 fails. So some numbers lead to fixed points? 72 leads to 78 fixed. 72 is even but not divisible by 4? Actually 72 = 2^3 * 3^2, exponent of 2 is 3, so divisible by 4. So that's not a fixed point. But it leads to 78 (fixed). So we need to determine all a1 such that the orbit stays within numbers with at least three proper divisors. Since f(N) can be larger, but eventually maybe they become fixed points? But there might be numbers that go to primes or to numbers with <3 proper divisors after a few steps. So we need to characterize a1.

We can attempt to find all a1 such that the sequence is defined (i.e., each a_n has at least three proper divisors). Let's denote S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. Equivalent to N * (1/d2+1/d3+1/d4). Since d2,d3,d4 are the three smallest divisors >1, which are all >1. The reciprocals sum is something. For N to be "good", the iteration must not encounter a number with fewer than three proper divisors. Let's think about numbers that have fewer than three proper divisors. Those are numbers with at most 2 proper divisors. Let's list possibilities:

- 1 has 0 proper divisors.
- Prime numbers have exactly 1 proper divisor (1). So they have <3.
- Square of a prime p^2 has proper divisors 1 and p (two). So they have exactly 2 proper divisors.
- Higher powers of primes: p^k with k>=3 has proper divisors 1,p,p^2,...,p^{k-1} which are at least 3 (for k=3, 1,p,p^2). So p^3 has 3 proper divisors.
- Numbers with many divisors (composite with at least 4 divisors) have at least 3 proper divisors, but careful: numbers like 6 have 3 proper divisors (1,2,3). So they qualify. But numbers like 4 (2^2) have 2 proper divisors, fails. Numbers like 9 (3^2) have 2 proper divisors, fails. Numbers like 25 (5^2) fail. Also numbers like 49 (7^2) fail. So the problematic numbers are those that are either prime, or a square of a prime (including squares of primes). Also numbers that are prime powers with exponent 2 are squares of primes. Also numbers like 1, but that's not relevant.

But also there are numbers with exactly 3 proper divisors? Actually if a number has exactly 3 proper divisors, then it's composite with exactly 3 proper divisors. Those are numbers like 6,8,10,14,15,21,22,26,27? Let's compute: 6 has 3 proper divisors (1,2,3). 8 has 3 (1,2,4). 10 has 3 (1,2,5). 14 has 3 (1,2,7). 15 has 3 (1,3,5). 21 has 3 (1,3,7). 22 has 3 (1,2,11). 26 has 3 (1,2,13). 27 has 3 (1,3,9). 30 has 3? Actually 30's proper divisors: 1,2,3,5,6,10,15 (that's 7). So 30 has many. So indeed many numbers have at least 3 proper divisors. So the "bad" numbers (with <3 proper divisors) are: primes, and squares of primes. Also numbers like 1? But 1 has no proper divisors, but it's not a positive integer with at least three proper divisors, so it's not allowed. But a_n must be positive integers with at least three proper divisors. So a_n cannot be 1, prime, or prime square. So the sequence must avoid those.

Now, the recursion is a_{n+1}=f(a_n). We need to find all a1 such that the entire orbit stays within numbers that are not primes and not squares of primes (and also not 1). But if a_n is composite with at least 3 proper divisors, then f(a_n) is defined. However we also need that f(a_n) itself has at least 3 proper divisors. So the condition is recursive.

So we need to determine all a1 for which the orbit is "good". This is a combinatorial number theory problem.

Let's attempt a systematic analysis.

Define S(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N. We'll compute S(N) based on prime factorization.

We need to find all positive integers a1 such that for all n≥1, a_n is composite with at least 3 proper divisors. Since the sequence is defined recursively, this is a property of the orbit. So we need to characterize the set of "good" numbers (those from which the sequence stays good). Let's denote G = { N ∈ ℕ⁺ | the sequence defined by a1 = N, a_{n+1}=S(a_n) is such that all a_n have at least 3 proper divisors }. Then we need to determine G.

Observations:

1. If N is prime or a square of a prime, then N has at most 2 proper divisors, so N ∉ G (since a1 itself fails). So G ⊆ {composite numbers with at least 3 proper divisors}.

2. If N is a fixed point (S(N)=N), then trivially N ∈ G, because then the sequence is constant N, and N has at least 3 proper divisors (by definition of fixed point we need to ensure N indeed has at least 3 proper divisors; but we will characterize fixed points as numbers N such that S(N)=N and N has at least 3 proper divisors. Actually if S(N)=N, then N must have at least 3 proper divisors (since we can sum three largest, so N has at least 3 proper divisors). So N ∈ G.

3. If N is such that S(N) is a fixed point and N itself has at least 3 proper divisors, then N ∈ G because the orbit becomes constant after one step. However, we must also ensure that S(N) has at least 3 proper divisors (which it does if it's a fixed point). So that's fine. So numbers that map to a fixed point are in G. Similarly, numbers that map to a number that eventually maps to a fixed point (without hitting bad numbers) are also in G, provided all intermediate numbers have at least 3 proper divisors.

Thus the problem reduces to finding all N such that the orbit under S never hits a number with <3 proper divisors. This is a kind of "invariant" or "stable" set.

We can try to prove that G is exactly the set of numbers that are multiples of 6 and are not divisible by 4 or 5? But 72 is a multiple of 6 and divisible by 4, yet it's in G. So that's not correct. Let's compute 72's orbit: 72 → 78 → 78 → ... So 72 ∈ G. So the condition is not that N is a fixed point. So we need to include numbers that map to a fixed point.

Now, which numbers map to a fixed point? Let's analyze S(N) for various N.

We have S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 if N is divisible by 2 and 3 and 4? Actually careful: The three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4 if N is divisible by 2 and 3 and 4. But does N need to be divisible by 4? The condition for the three smallest to be 2,3,4 is that N is divisible by 2 and 3, and also by 4. But if N is divisible by 2 and 3, it's divisible by 6. If N is divisible by 4, then it's divisible by 12. So for N divisible by 12, the smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,4 (since 4 is smaller than 6). Then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12. This is an integer because N/12 is integer. So S(N) = 13k where N = 12k. So S(N) = 13k.

Now, for N divisible by 12 but not necessarily by 3? Actually if N is divisible by 12, it's divisible by 3 and 4 and 2, so it's multiple of 6. So indeed.

Now, if N is divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4, then the three smallest are 2,3,6, and S(N) = N. So those are fixed points. So fixed points are precisely those N that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 (i.e., exponent of 2 is 1). However, they also need to not be divisible by 5? Wait earlier we derived that if N is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4, then the three smallest are 2,3,6, but only if N is not divisible by any integer between 2 and 6 exclusive other than 2 and 3. The only numbers between 2 and 6 exclusive are 3,4,5. So if N is not divisible by 4 or 5, then indeed the three smallest are 2,3,6. But if N is divisible by 5, then 5 is a divisor, and 5<6. So the three smallest would be 2,3,5. So S(N) would be N/2+N/3+N/5, which is not N. So such N are not fixed points. So fixed points are multiples of 6 with exponent of 2 = 1 and not divisible by 5. But also what about numbers that are multiples of 6 and have exponent of 2 = 1, but are divisible by 5? That's a subset of multiples of 6 with exponent 1, but they fail because they are not fixed points. However, they might map to something else. For example, N=30 is such. S(30)=31 (prime) fails. So not allowed.

Now, numbers that are multiples of 12 (i.e., divisible by 4 and 3) map to S(N) = 13N/12. So they map to a number that is 13 times k (where N=12k). So we can analyze when 13k is a fixed point. 13k is a fixed point if 13k is divisible by 6 (i.e., has 2 and 3), but also not divisible by 4 or 5. However, note that 13k may be a multiple of 6 only if k is a multiple of 6? Actually 13k is divisible by 2 and 3 if k is even? Let's compute: For 13k to be divisible by 2, k must be even (since 13 is odd). For 13k to be divisible by 3, k must be a multiple of 3. So 13k is divisible by 6 iff k is a multiple of 6. So if N=12k, then S(N) = 13k. So S(N) is a multiple of 6 iff k is a multiple of 6. If k is a multiple of 6, then S(N) is a multiple of 6. Moreover, S(N) will be a fixed point if also it is not divisible by 4 or 5. But we need to check whether S(N) is a fixed point (i.e., S(S(N)) = S(N)). That is equivalent to S(N) having the triple (2,3,6). So S(N) must be a multiple of 6, with exponent of 2 = 1 and not divisible by 5. So we need to find N such that N is divisible by 12, and S(N) = 13k is a fixed point. Then N ∈ G.

But also N could be such that S(N) is not a fixed point but eventually reaches a fixed point after more steps, and all intermediate numbers are good. For example, N=72 leads to S(N)=78 (fixed). That's covered. N=84 leads to 91, then 21, then ... fails. So N=84 not allowed.

Thus we need to characterize all a1 for which the orbit stays within numbers that have at least 3 proper divisors. Possibly the answer is: all a1 that are multiples of 6, except those that are multiples of 5 and not multiples of 4? Let's compute S(N) for N multiples of 6 with exponent of 2 = 1 (i.e., not divisible by 4) but divisible by 5: those are numbers like 30, 90, 150, 210, 270, etc. They are not fixed points. Now, multiples of 6 with exponent 1 and not divisible by 5 are allowed (fixed points). So the set of allowed a1 includes all multiples of 6 with exponent 1 and not divisible by 5. That's a set.

Now, what about multiples of 6 with exponent 1 but also divisible by 5? Not allowed. So some multiples of 6 are allowed, some not. But we also have numbers like 72 (exponent 3) that are allowed. So we need to determine all a1 such that the orbit is good.

What about numbers that are multiples of 6 but have exponent of 2 >=2, like 12,24,36,48,60,72,84,96,108,120,... Many fail. Some succeed. So need a more precise condition.

Yes, 234 is a fixed point. So 216 maps to 234 (fixed). 216 is allowed. So 216 works. So indeed many numbers with exponent 2 >=2 work as long as after one step they land on a fixed point. But some like 12,24,36,48,60,84,96,108,120,132,144,156,168,180,192, etc fail. So what distinguishes them? Let's analyze S(N) for N that are multiples of 6.

Let N be a multiple of 6. Write N = 2^a * 3^b * c, where c is an integer not divisible by 2 or 3. Then the smallest divisors >1 are determined by a and b.

We need to consider the three smallest divisors >1. The divisors are all numbers of the form 2^i * 3^j * d where d divides c. The smallest ones are those with small i and j. The candidates: 2, 3, 4 (if a≥2), 6, 8 (if a≥3), 9 (if b≥2), etc. So we need to compare values.

Let's list possible triples (d2,d3,d4) for N that are multiples of 6.

Case A: a = 1 (i.e., N has exactly one factor 2). Then N is even but not divisible by 4. Then 2 is a divisor. 3 is a divisor if b≥1 (since N is multiple of 6, b≥1). 4 is not a divisor. So the smallest divisors >1 are 2, 3, and then the next smallest could be 6 (since 6 is a divisor). But also if c has a prime factor p (like 5,7, etc.), then p could be a divisor. But note that p could be less than 6? The smallest possible primes >3 are 5,7,11,... So if c is divisible by 5, then 5 is a divisor, and 5<6. So the triple becomes 2,3,5. If c is not divisible by 5, then 6 is the next smallest divisor. Also if c is divisible by some number like 1? That's trivial. So for a=1, the triple is (2,3,6) iff N is not divisible by any integer d with 1<d<6 except 2 and 3, i.e., not divisible by 4 (already) and not divisible by 5. So that's the fixed point condition. If N is divisible by 5, then triple (2,3,5) and S(N) = N/2+N/3+N/5 = (15N+10N+6N)/30 = 31N/30. So S(N) = 31N/30. That is an integer if N is divisible by 30. Since N is multiple of 6, for N to be divisible by 30, N must be divisible by 5. So N = 30k. Then S(N) = 31k. So indeed.

Thus for a=1, we have subcases:
- N not divisible by 5 => S(N)=N (fixed).
- N divisible by 5 => S(N) = 31N/30 = 31k, where N=30k. Then S(N) may or may not be a fixed point, etc.

Now case B: a ≥ 2 (i.e., N divisible by 4). Then N is divisible by 4. Also N is divisible by 2 and 3, so N is multiple of 12. So N = 12 * t, where t is integer. The smallest divisors >1: 2,3,4. 4 is smaller than 6. So the triple is (2,3,4). Then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = (6N+4N+3N)/12 = 13N/12 = 13t. So S(N) = 13t, where N = 12t.

Now, for such N, S(N) may or may not be a fixed point, but we need to check whether the orbit stays good. If S(N) is a fixed point (i.e., S(N) is a multiple of 6 with exponent 1 and not divisible by 5), then N ∈ G. If S(N) is not a fixed point, then we need to examine further.

So the problem reduces to determining which a1 (N) produce an infinite sequence with each term having ≥3 proper divisors. Since the recursion is deterministic, we can analyze the orbit.

Observation: For any N that is a multiple of 6, if we apply S repeatedly, we might get numbers that are also multiples of 6 (maybe not always). For example, N=12 (multiple of 6) gives S=13 (not multiple of 6). So it's possible to leave multiples of 6. For N=72, S=78 which is multiple of 6. For N=216, S=234 which is multiple of 6. For N=30, S=31 (not multiple). So some multiples of 6 map to non-multiples. The condition for the orbit to be good is that all terms are composite with at least 3 proper divisors. So if any term becomes prime or a square of a prime, it fails. Also if any term becomes 1 (impossible). So we need to avoid those.

Now, we might try to characterize the set of numbers that are "bad" (i.e., have <3 proper divisors). Those are primes and squares of primes. The iteration from a good starting value might eventually become constant at a fixed point. Perhaps the only way to avoid a bad number is to have the orbit eventually become a fixed point, and that fixed point must be a number that is not a square of a prime (which is automatically satisfied because fixed points have at least 3 proper divisors). So we need to find all N such that the orbit under S never hits a prime or a prime square. That seems plausible.

Let's attempt to compute S for numbers that are not multiples of 6. Perhaps we can prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then S(N) is either a prime or a prime square, or that after a few steps we hit such. But we saw N=10 (not multiple of 6) gave S=8 which is not prime or prime square, but then 8 leads to 7 (prime). So it's not immediate. However, maybe we can prove that if N is not divisible by 6, then S(N) is less than N? N=9 has only 2 proper divisors, S not defined. N=25 has only 2 proper divisors. So not considered. Let's explore the function S for numbers that are multiples of 6 but not fixed points. Write N = 6 * k. Then we need to compute S(N) based on whether N is divisible by 4 or 5. But we can derive formulas:

- Let a = exponent of 2 in N. Then if a = 1, the candidates are 2,3,6, and also any divisor from c (primes) that are <6, i.e., 5. So if N is divisible by 5, then the third smallest is 5; else it's 6. Thus the behavior depends on the exponent of 2.

Now, the problem is to determine all possible a1. So we need to find all N such that the recursively defined sequence (a_n) satisfies that each a_n has at least 3 proper divisors. Since the recursion is deterministic, we can analyze the possible orbits.

Idea: Prove that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually becomes a prime (or prime square) within a few steps, which violates the condition. So a1 must be a multiple of 6. Now, can we simplify the description? Perhaps the set G can be described as: all numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6, and N is not of the form 2^a * 3^b * m where m is divisible by 5? That doesn't capture the distinction. Let's try to find a more elegant characterization. Observe that if N is a multiple of 6, then S(N) is:

- If v2(N) = 1 and 5 ∤ N, then S(N) = N.
- If v2(N) = 1 and 5 ∣ N, then S(N) = (31/30) N.
- If v2(N) ≥ 2, then S(N) = (13/12) N, but only if N is divisible by 12. For N multiple of 6 with v2(N)≥2, indeed N is divisible by 12, because v2(N)≥2 means N is divisible by 4, and also divisible by 3, so divisible by 12. So S(N) = 13N/12.

Now, we can try to determine all N such that the orbit stays within numbers with at least 3 proper divisors. This is reminiscent of iterating a function that can be expressed as rational multiples. The function f(N) = N * (1/d2+1/d3+1/d4). Since d2,d3,d4 are divisors, f(N) is integer. For N that are not multiples of 6, we need to prove that eventually a term becomes prime or prime square. Let's attempt to prove that if N is not a multiple of 6, then f(N) < N, and also that f(N) is either prime or a prime square, or that it leads to such. Let's analyze the residues systematically.

Let N be an integer with at least 3 proper divisors. Then f(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4. Write d2 = p (smallest prime factor). Then d3 is either p^2 (if p^2 divides N) or the second smallest prime factor q (if p^2 does not divide N). Similarly, d4 is determined.

Now, consider the prime factorization. We'll handle cases based on p.

Case p = 2. Then N is even. Since N is not a multiple of 6, 3 ∤ N. So N is even but not divisible by 3. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are:

- d2 = 2.
- d3: if N is divisible by 4, then d3 = 4; otherwise, N is not divisible by 4, so N = 2 * m with m odd, and the next smallest divisor is the smallest odd prime factor of m, call it r. Since m is odd and not divisible by 3, r ≥ 5. So d3 = r.
- d4: if N is divisible by 4, then d4 is either 8 (if N divisible by 8) or 5 (if N divisible by 5) or something like that. If N is not divisible by 4, then after 2 and r, the next smallest divisor could be r^2 (if r^2 divides N) or another prime s (≥ r). So we have subcases.

Now, we want to prove that the residues mod 3 of N/2, N/d3, N/d4 are not all equal. Let's try to compute these residues in terms of N and the divisors.

Since N is not divisible by 3, N ≡ ±1 (mod 3). For any divisor d of N that is not a multiple of 3, N/d mod 3 is (N mod 3) * (d^{-1} mod 3). So it's either (N mod 3) if d ≡ 1 (mod 3), or (N mod 3) * 2 if d ≡ 2 (mod 3). So the residue depends on d mod 3.

Now, we need to analyze the residues of d2,d3,d4 modulo 3. Since N is not a multiple of 3, none of its divisors are multiples of 3. So d2,d3,d4 are not multiples of 3. So they are either ≡ 1 or 2 mod 3.

Now, note that d2 = 2 ≡ 2 (mod 3). So N/2 ≡ (N mod 3) * (2^{-1}) ≡ (N mod 3) * 2 (since 2^{-1} mod 3 is 2). So N/2 ≡ 2*(N mod 3) ≡ (if N mod 3 = 1 → 2; if N mod 3 = 2 → 4 ≡ 1). So N/2 mod 3 is either 1 or 2, depending on N. Specifically, N/2 ≡ (2N) mod 3, but since N mod 3 is ±1, 2N mod 3 is ±2 ≡ ±2 mod 3. So it's not 0. So it's either 1 or 2.

Now, what about d3? If N is divisible by 4, then d3 = 4 ≡ 1 mod 3. Then N/4 ≡ N mod 3. So N/4 ≡ ±1. If N is not divisible by 4, then d3 is an odd prime r (≥5). r mod 3 could be 1 or 2. So N/r mod 3 is (N mod 3)*(r^{-1}) mod 3. r^{-1} mod 3 is 1 if r ≡ 1, or 2 if r ≡ 2. So N/r ≡ ±1 (depending). So each term is ±1 mod 3. So the residues are either 1 or 2. The sum of three numbers each ±1 can be 0 mod 3 if they are all 1 or all 2. So we need to see whether the three residues can be all equal. For N even not multiple of 3, we need to examine the possibilities.

Now, a = 1 means N is even but not divisible by 4. Write N = 2 * m, with m odd. Since N is not a multiple of 6, 3 ∤ N, so 3 ∤ m. The three smallest divisors >1 are:

- d4: we need to consider the third smallest divisor >1. Since N is not divisible by 4, the powers of 2 are only 2. So after 2, we have p. Then the next smallest divisor could be either:

Case B: d4 = q, where q is the second smallest odd prime factor of m, and q < 2p. Then we need to compute N/d4 = (2 m)/q = 2 (m/q). Since q divides m, write m = q * r, where r is integer. So S(N) is odd, hence not a multiple of 2, so cannot be a multiple of 6. So case B yields S(N) odd, not multiple of 6.

Thus, for a = 1, S(N) is either even and not divisible by 3 (case A) or odd (case B). In both cases, S(N) is not a multiple of 6. Therefore, we have proven:

**Lemma**: If N ≥ 2 has at least three proper divisors, then 6 ∤ N ⇒ 6 ∤ S(N).

Proof: Cases as above. We should also note that when N is even and not a multiple of 3, we have a ≥ 1. We covered a ≥ 2 and a = 1. For a ≥ 2, we proved S(N) not divisible by 6. For a = 1, we analyzed subcases and concluded S(N) not a multiple of 6. The argument for a = 1 required that we correctly identify d4. But is it possible that 2p < q and also that p^2 < q? Actually we already considered d4 = min(2p, q). So if q < 2p, then d4 = q. If q > 2p, then d4 = 2p. So the classification is correct. However, we also need to consider the possibility that m has a factor p^2 and no other odd primes, then d4 = 2p. That's covered in case A. If m has factor p^2 and also another odd prime q, then both p^2 and q are divisors. But p^2 is > 2p (since p>2), and q may be less than 2p. So the third smallest is min(2p, q). So still either 2p or q. So our cases cover all possibilities.

Now, also need to consider the scenario where m has only one odd prime factor p, but the exponent is ≥ 2, so m = p^k. Then the divisors of N are: 2, p, p^2, ..., p^k, and also 2p, 2p^2, etc. The smallest divisors >1 are 2, p, then what's the third? 2p is a divisor. Is it smaller than p^2? For p≥3, 2p < p^2 (since p^2-2p = p(p-2) > 0). So the third smallest is 2p. So d4 = 2p. That's case A. So indeed, case A covers all situations where the third smallest is 2p.

Now, for a = 1, we also need to consider the possibility that m is a prime power, and the exponent is 1 (i.e., m is prime). Then m = p (prime). Then N = 2p. The divisors are 1,2,p,2p. Proper divisors are 1,2,p. So there are three proper divisors. The three largest proper divisors are p,2,1. That's S(N) = p+2+1 = p+3. So S(N) = p+3. Since p is odd, p+3 is even. So S(N) is even. Also, we need to check if S(N) is divisible by 3. For p=5, S=8; p=11, S=14; p=17, S=20; p=23, S=26; p=29, S=32; etc. None are divisible by 3. But is there a prime p such that p+3 is divisible by 3? Now, for a = 1 and m has at least two distinct odd primes, we need to check that in case B, d4 = q (second smallest odd prime) and that q < 2p, but also that 2p is a divisor, and we have d4 = min(2p, q). So S(N) is odd. So not a multiple of 6.

Thus, the lemma is proven.

Now, we can use this lemma to show that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence cannot be all with at least 3 proper divisors. Because we can prove by induction that all a_n are not multiples of 6. But we need to be careful: The lemma says that if N has at least 3 proper divisors and 6 ∤ N, then S(N) is not a multiple of 6. So if we assume that a_n has at least 3 proper divisors and is not a multiple of 6, then a_{n+1} = S(a_n) is not a multiple of 6. - If N is odd, then S(N) is odd, but is it < N? We need to examine. For odd N, we have S(N) = N/d2 + N/d3 + N/d4. Since d2 ≥ 3 (odd), and N/d2 ≤ N/3, N/d3 ≤ N/5, etc. So S(N) ≤ N/3 + N/5 + N/7? We can use the same case analysis as before, but now we want to prove that if 6 ∤ N and N has at least 3 proper divisors, then S(N) < N. Actually we already have a lemma that S(N) is not a multiple of 6. That doesn't directly imply S(N) < N. So indeed, S(N) ≤ N/3+N/5+N/7. That's a valid inequality. So S(N) ≤ (71/105)N < N. So S(N) < N.

Now, take N even but not a multiple of 3. We'll need to prove S(N) < N. For N even, we already have a case analysis based on a (exponent of 2). We can bound S(N) similarly.

- If a ≥ 2, we have S(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/d4. Since N is divisible by 4. Then S(N) ≤ N/2+N/4+N/8 (if a≥3, d4=8) or N/2+N/4+N/p (if a=2). But we can bound S(N) ≤ N/2+N/4+N/8 = 7N/8 < N. But we need an inequality that S < N. Since N = 4 m, and S = t(3p+4). We can try to prove S ≤ (3p+4)/4 * m? Actually N = 4 m. So we need to show S < N. Compare S and N. S = t(3p+4). Since m = p t, N = 4 p t. So S/N = t(3p+4) / (4 p t) = (3p+4) / (4p) = 3/4 + 1/p. For p≥5, this is ≤ 3/4 + 1/5 = 0.75 + 0.2 = 0.95. So S ≤ 0.95 N < N. That's a valid bound, but we need to ensure that S is integer and that S < N. Since (3p+4)/(4p) < 1 for p>0. So S ≤ floor? Actually S = t(3p+4). N = 4 p t. So S/N = (3p+4)/(4p). Since S and N are integers, we can multiply. For any t, S/N = (3p+4)/(4p). This is less than 1. So S < N. That's a direct comparison without bounding by the sum of reciprocals. Indeed, S = t(3p+4). N = 4 p t. Since (3p+4) < 4p for p≥? Compute 4p - (3p+4) = p-4. For p≥5, p-4 ≥1, so 3p+4 ≤ 4p-1 < 4p. Hence, S < N. So for a=2, S < N. For a≥3, S = 7N/8 < N. So indeed, S(N) < N for all N even not multiple of 3.

Thus, we have:

- If N is odd (and has ≥3 proper divisors), S(N) < N.
- If N is even and not a multiple of 3, S(N) < N.

Now, the lemma we proved earlier says that if N is not a multiple of 6, then S(N) is not a multiple of 6. So we can use these to argue:

Assume a1 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can prove by induction that for all n, a_n is not a multiple of 6. Because if a_n is not a multiple of 6, then by the lemma, a_{n+1} is not a multiple of 6. Also, we need to ensure that the sequence is defined (a_n has ≥3 proper divisors). But we are proving that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then it's impossible for the sequence to be defined for all n with each a_n having ≥3 proper divisors. So we assume, for contradiction, that such a sequence exists (all a_n have ≥3 proper divisors). Then a1 is not a multiple of 6. Then a2 = S(a1) is not a multiple of 6 (by lemma). Similarly, a3 is not a multiple of 6, etc. So all a_n are not multiples of 6. Also, from the bound, S(N) < N for N not a multiple of 6, so the sequence is strictly decreasing. Since it's strictly decreasing and all terms are positive integers, it must eventually become ≤ 6. But we also have that all a_n are not multiples of 6. Therefore, eventually a_n ≤ 6 and a_n ≠ 6. The only possible values ≤ 6 that are not multiples of 6 are 1,2,3,4,5. All of those have fewer than 3 proper divisors. But a_n must have at least 3 proper divisors (by definition of the sequence). Contradiction. Hence, a1 must be a multiple of 6.

Thus, necessity is proven.

Now, we need to determine all possible a1 that are multiples of 6. But not all multiples of 6 work. So we need to find the subset of multiples of 6 for which the sequence is good. We'll do that by analyzing the orbit for a1 that is a multiple of 6.

Let a1 = N, 6 | N. Then define a2 = S(N). We want to know whether the sequence can consist of numbers each with at least 3 proper divisors. We'll need to examine the possible behavior.

We can try to prove that if N is a multiple of 6, then the sequence is good if and only if either (i) v2(N) = 1 and 5 ∤ N, or (ii) v2(N) ≥ 3 and N/72 is odd and 5 ∤ N/72. But we need to also consider the possibility that N is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 4, i.e., v2(N)=1. Those are in case (i). If v2(N)≥2, then N is divisible by 4, so N is a multiple of 12. Then we can consider subcases. But we also need to consider the possibility that N is a multiple of 6 and v2(N)≥2 but N is not a multiple of 72. Then a2 = 13N/12 (since triple (2,3,4)). But we need to check if a2 has at least 3 proper divisors. If a2 is prime or prime square, then it fails. But we can analyze the orbit: a2 will be odd (if N/12 is odd) or even (if N/12 is even). Let's test.

If N is a multiple of 12 but not of 72, then N/12 is not a multiple of 6. In fact, N/12 is an integer. Then a2 = 13 * (N/12). Since N/12 is integer, a2 is integer. We need to check whether a2 is a multiple of 6. If N/12 is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. But could a2 have at least 3 proper divisors? It might. However, we can try to prove that if N is a multiple of 6 and not in the two types, then the sequence will eventually encounter a term with <3 proper divisors. We can do this by induction or by analyzing the forward orbit.

But we already have a contradiction argument: If a1 is not a multiple of 6, then it's impossible. So a1 must be a multiple of 6. Now, we need to find all multiples of 6 for which the sequence is good. That is, we need to characterize the set G.

We can attempt to prove:

- If N is a multiple of 6 and also N is a fixed point (i.e., S(N)=N) and N has at least 3 proper divisors, then obviously the sequence is constant, good.
- If N is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point, then we need to examine whether the orbit stays good.

We can also try to prove that the only way for the sequence to be infinite with all terms having ≥3 proper divisors is if a1 is a multiple of 6 and the sequence becomes constant after at most 2 steps. That's a kind of "eventually constant" condition. So we can try to prove that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and the sequence is good, then a2 is a fixed point (or a1 itself is a fixed point). And then we can classify.

But we saw that a1=72 is not a fixed point, but a2=78 is fixed, so indeed the sequence becomes constant after one step. So that fits.

Now, we need to prove that if a1 is a multiple of 6 and the sequence is good, then a2 must be a fixed point. However, is it possible that a1 is a multiple of 6, a2 is not a fixed point, but a3 is a fixed point, and the sequence is good? That would be a longer chain. For example, a1=12, a2=13 (prime), not good. a1=36, a2=39 (composite), a3=25 (prime square), fails. a1=48, a2=52, a3=91, a4=21, a5=11, fails. a1=60, a2=65, a3=19, fails. a1=84, a2=91, a3=21, a4=11, fails. a1=96, a2=104, a3=91, fails. a1=108, a2=117, a3=61, fails. a1=120, a2=130, a3=104, fails. So far no example where a2 is not fixed but a3 is fixed and all are good. Could there be a chain where a2 is not fixed, but a3 is fixed, and all terms have ≥3 proper divisors? We can attempt to construct. Suppose N is multiple of 6. Then a2 = 13N/12 if N is multiple of 12. For a2 to be a fixed point, we need that a2 is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5. That gave condition (ii). If N is multiple of 6 but not multiple of 12, then v2(N)=1, so N is of the form 2 * 3 * m with m odd. Then a2 = N if 5 ∤ N, else a2 = 31N/30. So if a2 is not fixed, it's either 31N/30. Then we can compute a3 = S(a2). That might be something else. So it's plausible that the only good a1 are those that map to a fixed point in one step. But we need a proof.

We can do a case analysis based on the exponent of 2 in a1. Since a1 is a multiple of 6. Write a1 = 2^a * 3^b * c, with c odd and not divisible by 2 or 3. Since a1 is multiple of 6, we have a ≥ 1, b ≥ 1. Then:

- If a = 1 and b ≥ 1. Then a1 is divisible by 2 and 3 but not by 4. So a1 = 2 * 3 * m, with m odd, and 3 ∤ m? Actually we can factor out 3^b. Let's keep as a1 = 2 * 3 * k, where k is odd and may contain powers of 3? Actually if b≥2, then a1 has factor 3^2 = 9. But then a1 is divisible by 2 and 9, but not by 4. That's allowed. So a1 = 2 * 3^b * c, with c odd and not divisible by 2 or 3. Then we can compute S(a1) based on whether 5 divides a1. The triple (d2,d3,d4) depends on whether a1 is divisible by 4 (it's not) and whether it's divisible by 5. So we have:

   * If 5 ∤ a1, then d2=2, d3=3 (since 3 divides a1), d4=6 (since 6 divides a1). Then S(a1)=a1 (fixed point). So such a1 are good.
   * If 5 ∣ a1, then d2=2, d3=3, d4=5 (since 5 < 6). Then S(a1)=a1/2+a1/3+a1/5 = (31/30)a1. Since a1 is a multiple of 30 (because it's multiple of 6 and 5), we have a2 = 31 * (a1/30). Then a2 is odd. We need to check whether a2 has at least 3 proper divisors. Possibly it does, but then the sequence will decrease. We can analyze a2's image. We'll need to prove that such a1 are not good. That is, if a1 is a multiple of 6 and also a multiple of 5, then the sequence fails. We'll need to prove that.

- If a ≥ 2. Then a1 is divisible by 4. Since a1 is multiple of 6, it's divisible by 12. So a1 = 12 * t, where t is integer. Then we compute S(a1) = a1/2 + a1/3 + a1/4 = 13N/12 = 13t. So a2 = 13t. Now, we need to determine whether the sequence is good. That depends on t. If t is a multiple of 6, then a2 is a multiple of 6. Then we need to see if a2 is a fixed point or not. But if t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then the sequence will eventually fail because it will strictly decrease from a2 onward. So such a1 are not good. Thus, a1 must be such that t is a multiple of 6. That is, a1 is divisible by 72. Then a2 = 13 * (a1/12) = 13 * (6 * (a1/72)) = 78 * (a1/72). So a2 = 78 * u, where u = a1/72. Now, a2 is a multiple of 6. For the sequence to be good, we need that a2 is a fixed point, i.e., v2(a2)=1 and 5 ∤ a2. As computed, v2(a2) = 1 + v2(u). So we need v2(u)=0, i.e., u odd. Also, 5 ∤ a2 iff 5 ∤ u. So we need u odd and not divisible by 5. That's the condition.

Thus, the good a1 are those with:

- a1 ≡ 0 mod 6.
- Either (i) a1 is not divisible by 4 (i.e., v2(a1)=1) and a1 is not divisible by 5, or
- (ii) a1 is divisible by 72 and a1/72 is odd and not divisible by 5.

But note: In case (i), a1 is not divisible by 4, so v2(a1)=1. Also, a1 is not divisible by 5. However, is it possible that a1 is not divisible by 4 and also not divisible by 5, but is divisible by 3 and 2, so it's a multiple of 6. That's exactly the fixed points. But we also need to consider that a1 might have v2(a1)=1 and also be divisible by 5? That's not good. So the condition is necessary and sufficient.

Now, we also need to verify that if a1 satisfies (i), then a1 itself is a fixed point, so the sequence is constant. That's good. If a1 satisfies (ii), then a2 is a fixed point, so after one step it becomes constant. So those are good.

But we also need to check that a1 itself has at least 3 proper divisors. In (i), a1 is a multiple of 6, so it has at least 4 divisors, so condition holds. In (ii), a1 is a multiple of 72, so also has many divisors. So all good.

Now, are there any a1 that are multiples of 6 but not in (i) or (ii) that still produce an infinite good sequence? For example, a1 = 6 * something that is not a multiple of 4, but also not a multiple of 5. That's (i). For a1 = 72 * something that is even, then a2 is not a fixed point, but a2 might be such that its image a3 becomes fixed and all terms have ≥3 proper divisors. Let's examine a1 = 144 (which is 72*2). We saw a2=156, a3=169, fails. a1 = 216 (72*3) works. a1 = 360 (72*5) fails. So indeed only those with odd u and not divisible by 5 work. But could there be a1 that is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 72, yet a2 is not a multiple of 6, but a2 has at least 3 proper divisors and then a3 becomes fixed (maybe) without hitting a bad number? We need to check.

Take a1 = 12 (multiple of 6, not multiple of 72). a2 = 13 (prime). Fails. a1 = 36 → a2 = 39 (composite, but a3=25 fails). a1 = 48 → a2 = 52, a3 = 91, a4=21 fails. a1 = 60 → a2 = 65, a3=19 fails. a1 = 84 → a2=91, a3=21 fails. a1 = 96 → a2=104, a3=91, fails. So seems they all fail. But we need a proof. We can argue: If a1 is a multiple of 6 and v2(a1) ≥ 2, then a1 is divisible by 12. Write a1 = 12 * t. Then a2 = 13t. Now, if t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can apply the decreasing argument to a2: Since a2 is not a multiple of 6 (and we assume that a2 has at least 3 proper divisors, as it's part of the sequence), then by the lemma, a3 = S(a2) is not a multiple of 6 and is smaller than a2. So the sequence will strictly decrease from a2 onward. Eventually, it will hit a term ≤ 6 that is not a multiple of 6, which cannot have ≥3 proper divisors. Thus, such a1 cannot be good. So indeed, for a1 to be good, we must have t multiple of 6. That is, a1 divisible by 72. So that's necessary.

Similarly, if a1 is not divisible by 4, then a1 = 2 * 3 * m with m odd, and we have two subcases: if 5 ∤ a1, then a2 = a1 (fixed point), good; if 5 ∣ a1, then a2 = 31 * (a1/30). Now, a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can apply the decreasing argument to a2. Since a2 is not a multiple of 6, and we assume that a2 has at least 3 proper divisors (as it's part of the sequence), then by the lemma, a3 is not a multiple of 6 and smaller. So the sequence will strictly decrease from a2 onward. Eventually, it will hit a term ≤ 6 that is not a multiple of 6, contradiction. Thus, such a1 cannot be good. Therefore, if a1 is a multiple of 6 and not in (i) or (ii), then the sequence fails. So the only possible a1 are those in (i) or (ii).

Thus, the answer set is:

- All numbers that are multiples of 6, are not divisible by 4, and are not divisible by 5.
- All numbers that are multiples of 72, and such that the quotient (a1/72) is odd and not divisible by 5.

We can also express this more compactly:

Let a1 be a positive integer. Then the infinite sequence exists if and only if either

   (1) a1 is a multiple of 6, not a multiple of 4, and not a multiple of 5; or
   (2) a1 is a multiple of 72, and a1/72 is odd and not a multiple of 5.

But we should also consider the possibility that a1 is a multiple of 6 and also is a multiple of 4, but a1/72 is even, yet maybe a2 is a multiple of 6 and not fixed, but a3 becomes fixed and all terms have ≥3 proper divisors. We argued that if a1 is divisible by 12 but not by 72, then a2 is not a multiple of 6, so decreasing argument applies. But could it be that a2 is a multiple of 6 (if t is multiple of 6) but a1 is not divisible by 72? Wait if a1 is divisible by 12, then t = a1/12. If t is a multiple of 6, then a1/12 is multiple of 6 => a1 is multiple of 72. So indeed, if a1 is divisible by 12 and t is multiple of 6, then a1 is divisible by 72. So that's covered. So any a1 that is a multiple of 12 but not a multiple of 72 leads to a2 not a multiple of 6. So fails.

Now, we should also consider a1 that is a multiple of 6 but not a multiple of 12, i.e., v2(a1)=1. That's the case where a1 is even but not divisible by 4. Then a2 is either a1 (if 5 ∤ a1) or 31*(a1/30). In the latter case, a2 is not a multiple of 6. So fails. So only those with 5 ∤ a1 are good. So that's (i). But is it possible that a1 is a multiple of 6 and v2(a1)=1, and also a1 is a multiple of 5, but then a2 is not a multiple of 6, and then a3 becomes a fixed point? For that to happen, we would need that a2 is such that its image a3 is a fixed point and all intermediate terms have ≥3 proper divisors. But a2 is not a multiple of 6, so by the lemma, a3 is not a multiple of 6 and is smaller. So it's decreasing. It cannot become a fixed point (which is a multiple of 6) later. So it will strictly decrease. So fails. So indeed, those are not good.

Thus, the classification is correct.

Now, we need to present the solution in a clear, rigorous way. Let's outline the solution:

1. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors of N, assuming N has at least three proper divisors.
2. The sequence a_n is defined by a_1 = N, a_{n+1} = f(a_n). The condition says each a_n has at least three proper divisors, so f is defined for all n.
3. Goal: Determine all possible N (a1) for which such a sequence exists.

We'll prove:

**Lemma 1**: For any integer N ≥ 2 with at least three proper divisors,
   - If N is odd, then f(N) is odd.
   - If N is even and not a multiple of 3, then f(N) is not a multiple of 6 (i.e., it is not both even and divisible by 3). Actually we need a lemma that f(N) is not a multiple of 6 when N is not a multiple of 6. We'll prove that using case analysis.

But we already have a combined lemma that if N has at least three proper divisors and 6 ∤ N, then 6 ∤ f(N). Let's prove that.

We can break into cases:

- N odd → f(N) odd → not multiple of 6.
- N even, 3 ∤ N. Write N = 2^a * m, with m odd, a ≥ 1. Since 3 ∤ N, m not divisible by 3. Then consider a:
   * If a ≥ 2: N divisible by 4. Then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2, 4, and either 8 (if a≥3) or the smallest odd divisor p (if a=2). In both subcases, we can compute f(N) and show it's not a multiple of 6. We can present a unified argument: For a ≥ 2, f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/d, where d is the third smallest divisor. Then we can show that f(N) is not divisible by 3 (by evaluating modulo 3) and also that f(N) is not necessarily even but we need to show that 6 ∤ f(N). Actually if f(N) is odd, then it's not multiple of 6. If it's even, we need to show it's not divisible by 3. So we can compute f(N) modulo 3. But careful: For a ≥ 2, N is divisible by 4, so N is divisible by 2^2. In the case a=2, we have N = 4m. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/p = 2m + m + (4m)/p = 3m + 4t (where t = m/p). As we argued, t is odd (since m is odd, p is odd). So f(N) is odd. So not multiple of 6. For a ≥ 3, f(N) = N/2 + N/4 + N/8 = 7N/8. Then N is divisible by 8, so N is divisible by 2^3. Then f(N) is integer. We can check mod 3: N ≡ ±1 → f(N) ≡ 2N + N + 2N = 5N ≡ 2N (mod 3), not 0. So f(N) is not divisible by 3. Also, f(N) may be even or odd. But since it's not divisible by 3, it cannot be a multiple of 6. So indeed, for a ≥ 2, f(N) is not a multiple of 6.

   * If a = 1: N is even but not divisible by 4. Write N = 2m, m odd. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/p + N/d4, where p is the smallest odd prime factor of m, and d4 is the third smallest divisor. We need to analyze subcases. We already did that: d4 = 2p or q (second odd prime). In either case, we can compute f(N) and show it's not a multiple of 6. In the case d4 = 2p, f(N) = m + 2t + t = m + 3t = t(p+3) with t = m/p (odd). Then f(N) is even but not divisible by 3 (since p t ≠ 0 mod 3). In the case d4 = q, f(N) = v (p q + 2p + 2q) with v odd, and the factor (p q + 2p + 2q) is odd, so f(N) is odd. So f(N) is not a multiple of 6. Good.

Thus, the lemma is proven.

Now, with this lemma, we can show necessity: If a1 is not a multiple of 6, then by induction all a_n are not multiples of 6, and also S(N) < N for N not a multiple of 6, so the sequence is strictly decreasing. Then eventually a_n ≤ 6 and a_n ≠ 6, contradiction. So a1 must be a multiple of 6.

But is the "S(N) < N" part always true for N not a multiple of 6? We argued that for odd N, S(N) ≤ (71/105)N < N. For even N not multiple of 3, we argued that S(N) < N via direct comparison. Let's confirm for even N not multiple of 3 with a=1 (N even not divisible by 4). In that case, S(N) = t(p+3) with t odd, and N = 2 p t. Since p+3 < 2p for p≥5? Actually compare (p+3) and 2p. For p=5, p+3=8, 2p=10 → 8<10. For p=11, 14<22. For p=17, 20<34. So (p+3) < 2p, thus S(N) = t(p+3) < t * 2p = N. So S(N) < N. For a=2, S(N) = t(3p+4) with N=4p t. Compare (3p+4) and 4p: 4p - (3p+4) = p-4 ≥ 1 for p≥5. So S(N) < N. For a≥3, S(N)=7N/8 < N. So indeed, S(N) < N for all N not multiple of 6 (with ≥3 proper divisors). Good.

Thus, the decreasing argument works.

Now, for a1 that are multiples of 6, we need to determine which ones are good. We'll do a case analysis based on the exponent of 2 in a1.

Let N = a1. Since 6|N, N = 2^a * 3^b * c, with c odd and not divisible by 2 or 3. a ≥ 1, b ≥ 1.

We'll consider two main cases: a = 1 and a ≥ 2.

Case a = 1: N is even but not divisible by 4. Then N = 2 * 3^b * c, with c odd, not divisible by 2 or 3. Then:

- If 5 ∤ N, then the three smallest divisors >1 are 2, 3, 6. Then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/6 = N. So a2 = N, constant, good.
- If 5 ∣ N, then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/5 = (31/30)N. Since N is a multiple of 30, write N = 30 * s. Then a2 = 31 * s. Now, a2 is odd. Also, a2 has at least three proper divisors? It might or might not. But we need to show that such N cannot be good. Because we already have a contradiction argument: If a1 is good, then all terms are good. For a1 with a=1 and 5|N, we can show that a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then by the lemma, a3 is not a multiple of 6 and a3 < a2. So the sequence strictly decreases from a2 onward. Eventually it will encounter a term ≤ 6 that is not 6, contradiction. So such N are not good.

But is it possible that a2 is a multiple of 6? Let's compute a2 = 31 * s. For a2 to be a multiple of 6, we need s to be a multiple of 6 (since 31 is not). But s = N/30. Since N is multiple of 6, N/30 might be integer. Could s be a multiple of 6? That would require N to be a multiple of 180. But then a2 = 31 * (multiple of 6) is a multiple of 6? 31 * (6k) = 186k, which is divisible by 6. So a2 could be a multiple of 6 if s is a multiple of 6. However, does such N exist? For N to be a multiple of 6 and also a multiple of 5, N must be a multiple of 30. If N is a multiple of 30 and also a multiple of 6 (which is automatic), and we consider a=1 case (N not divisible by 4), can N be a multiple of 30 and also not divisible by 4? Yes, e.g., N = 30 itself, a=1? 30 is divisible by 2, but not by 4, so a=1. N=30 is not a multiple of 4, so a=1. N=30, s = 1. a2 = 31. Not multiple of 6. N=90, s=3. a2=93, not multiple. N=150, s=5. a2=155. Not multiple. N=210, s=7. a2=217. Not multiple. N=270, s=9. a2=279. Not multiple. N=330, s=11. a2=341. Not multiple. So s is odd, not multiple of 6. Could there be N with a=1 and 5|N such that s is a multiple of 6? That would require N to be a multiple of 180. But then N is divisible by 180 = 2^2 * 3^2 * 5. Since 180 has 2^2, N would be divisible by 4. That contradicts a=1. So indeed, if a=1, then N is not divisible by 4, so it cannot be divisible by 4, thus cannot be divisible by 180 (which has factor 4). So s cannot be a multiple of 6. So a2 is not a multiple of 6. Good. So our argument holds.

Thus, in case a=1, the only good N are those with 5 ∤ N. (We also need to consider that N must have at least 3 proper divisors, which holds because N is a multiple of 6.)

Now, case a ≥ 2: N is divisible by 4. Since N is a multiple of 6, it is divisible by 12. So N = 12 * t, t integer. Then S(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/4 = 13t. So a2 = 13t.

Now, if t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then we can apply the decreasing argument to a2 (since a2 has at least 3 proper divisors as part of the sequence). But we need to be careful: We are trying to determine whether N is good. If t is not a multiple of 6, then a2 is not a multiple of 6. Then by the lemma, a3 = S(a2) is not a multiple of 6 and is smaller. So the sequence will strictly decrease from a2 onward, eventually hitting a term ≤ 6 that is not 6, contradiction. Therefore, such N cannot be good. So for N to be good, we must have t a multiple of 6, i.e., N divisible by 72.

Thus, a ≥ 2 and N divisible by 72. Then a2 = 13 * (N/12) = 13 * (6 * (N/72)) = 78 * u, where u = N/72. So a2 = 78 u.

Now, we need to check whether the sequence is good. a2 is a multiple of 6, so we need to examine its behavior. If a2 is a fixed point, then the sequence becomes constant. a2 is a fixed point if and only if v2(a2) = 1 and 5 ∤ a2. Compute v2(a2) = v2(78 u) = v2(78) + v2(u) = 1 + v2(u). So v2(a2) = 1 iff v2(u) = 0, i.e., u is odd. Also, a2 is not divisible by 5 iff 5 ∤ u. Therefore, if u is odd and not divisible by 5, then a2 is a fixed point, and the sequence is good. If u is even or divisible by 5, then a2 is not a fixed point. But can the sequence still be good? We need to analyze further. Let's consider subcases:

- If u is even, then a2 has v2 ≥ 2, so it is not a fixed point. Moreover, a2 is a multiple of 6 but not a fixed point. Then we can examine its image a3 = S(a2). Since a2 is a multiple of 6, we can apply the same analysis to a2. But note that a2 may be such that its image is a fixed point. However, we need to check if the sequence can be all good. We can try to prove that if a2 is not a fixed point, then the sequence will eventually encounter a term with <3 proper divisors. But maybe we can argue by induction: If a1 is good, then a2 must be a fixed point. But is that necessarily true? Let's test with a1 = 72 (u=1 odd, not divisible by 5) works. a1 = 216 (u=3 odd, not divisible by 5) works. a1 = 360 (u=5, odd but divisible by 5) fails. a1 = 144 (u=2 even) fails. ## Solution
We are asked: Determine all possible values of \(a_1\) such that the infinite sequence \(a_1,a_2,\dots\) consists of positive integers, each having at least three proper divisors, and for every \(n\ge 1\) the integer \(a_{n+1}\) equals the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(a_n\).

**Notation.**  
For an integer \(N\ge 2\) we denote by \(S(N)\) the sum of its three largest proper divisors, provided \(N\) has at least three proper divisors; otherwise \(S(N)\) is undefined.  
The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=S(a_n)\) for all \(n\ge 1\).  The problem’s hypothesis is that every term has at least three proper divisors, so \(S\) is defined for all indices.

---

### 1.  A useful lemma  

**Lemma 1.**  Let \(N\ge 2\) have at least three proper divisors.  
If \(6\nmid N\) then \(6\nmid S(N)\).

*Proof.*  We consider two cases according to the parity of \(N\).

*Case 1. \(N\) is odd.*  
All divisors of \(N\) are odd; therefore the three smallest divisors \(>1\) are odd, and so are the three largest proper divisors (they are \(N/d_2,N/d_3,N/d_4\) with \(d_2,d_3,d_4\) odd).  The sum of three odd numbers is odd.  Hence \(S(N)\) is odd, consequently not a multiple of \(6\).

*Case 2. \(N\) is even.*  
Write the prime factorisation of \(N\) as  
\[
N = 2^a\cdot m,\qquad a\ge 1,\; m\ \text{odd},\; 3\nmid N\ (\text{otherwise }6\mid N\text{ and we are not in this case}).
\]  
Because \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\), we have \(3\nmid N\); thus \(3\nmid m\).

We need to examine the third largest proper divisor, which depends on the exponent \(a\) of \(2\).

#### 2.1  Subcase \(a\ge 2\)  

Here \(N\) is divisible by \(4\).  The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2,4\) and either \(8\) (if \(a\ge 3\)) or the smallest odd prime divisor \(p\) of \(m\) (if \(a=2\)).  
Consequently  
\[
S(N)=\begin{cases}
N/2+N/4+N/8 = 7N/8 & \text{if }a\ge 3,\\[2mm]
N/2+N/4+N/p = 2m+m+\dfrac{4m}{p} = 3m+\dfrac{4m}{p} & \text{if }a=2.
\end{cases}
\]

*If \(a\ge 3\):*  \(N\) is divisible by \(8\).  Because \(3\nmid N\), we have \(N\equiv \pm1\pmod 3\).  Then  
\[
\frac{N}{2}\equiv 2N,\quad \frac{N}{4}\equiv N,\quad \frac{N}{8}\equiv 2N\pmod 3,
\]  
and therefore  
\[
S(N)\equiv 2N+N+2N = 5N \equiv 2N \pmod 3.
\]  
Since \(2N\not\equiv0\pmod 3\), \(S(N)\) is not divisible by \(3\).  Hence \(6\nmid S(N)\).

*If \(a=2\):*  \(N=4m\) and the third smallest divisor is the smallest odd prime factor \(p\) of \(m\).  Write \(m=p\cdot t\) with \(t\) integer.  Then  
\[
S(N)=\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{4}+\frac{N}{p}=2m+m+\frac{4m}{p}=3m+\frac{4m}{p}.
\]  
Because \(p\mid m\), we have \(\frac{m}{p}=t\) integer.  Thus  
\[
S(N)=3p\,t+4t = t\,(3p+4).
\]  
Now \(t=m/p\) is odd (odd divided by odd is odd), and \(p\) is odd, so \(3p+4\) is odd.  Hence \(S(N)\) is odd.  Consequently \(6\nmid S(N)\) (it is not even).

Thus for every \(a\ge2\) we obtain \(6\nmid S(N)\).

#### 2.2  Subcase \(a=1\)  

Here \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(4\).  Write \(N=2\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd.  Since \(3\nmid N\), we have \(3\nmid m\).  The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2\), the smallest odd prime divisor \(p\) of \(m\), and a third divisor which is the minimum of \(2p\) and the second smallest odd prime divisor \(q\) (if any).  Therefore  
\[
S(N)=\begin{cases}
N/2+N/p+N/(2p)=m+\dfrac{2m}{p}+\dfrac{m}{p}=m+3\cdot\dfrac{m}{p} & \text{if } 2p\le q \text{ or no } q,\\[2mm]
N/2+N/p+N/q=m+\dfrac{2m}{p}+\dfrac{2m}{q} & \text{if } q<2p.
\end{cases}
\]

We handle the two possibilities separately.

*If the third divisor is \(2p\):*  then \(S(N)=m+3\cdot\frac{m}{p}\).  Because \(p\mid m\) and \(m\) is odd, \(\frac{m}{p}\) is odd.  Let \(t=\frac{m}{p}\) (odd).  Then \(S(N)=p\,t+3t = t\,(p+3)\).  
Now \(p\) is odd, so \(p+3\) is even.  Since \(3\nmid N\) and \(N=2p\,t\), we have \(3\nmid p\,t\); therefore \(t\,(p+3)\) is not divisible by \(3\) (because the factor \(t\,p\) is not a multiple of \(3\), and multiplying by the even factor \((p+3)\) does not introduce a factor \(3\)).  Hence \(S(N)\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), so \(6\nmid S(N)\).

*If the third divisor is \(q\) (a prime with \(q<2p\)):*  then \(S(N)=m+\dfrac{2m}{p}+\dfrac{2m}{q}\).  Since \(p\) and \(q\) divide \(m\), write \(m=p\,q\,v\) with \(v\) integer.  Then  
\[
S(N)=p\,q\,v+2q\,v+2p\,v = v\,(pq+2p+2q).
\]  
Now \(p,q\) are odd, so \(pq\) is odd; \(2p\) and \(2q\) are even.  Hence \(pq+2p+2q\) is odd.  Moreover \(v\) is odd (odd divided by odd is odd).  Consequently \(S(N)\) is odd.  Thus \(6\nmid S(N)\).

In either subcase of \(a=1\) we obtain \(6\nmid S(N)\).

---

We have examined all possibilities when \(6\nmid N\) and \(N\) has at least three proper divisors.  In every case \(S(N)\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  This completes the proof of Lemma 1. ∎



### 2.  Necessity of \(6\mid a_1\)

Assume that a sequence \(a_1,a_2,\dots\) with the required properties exists.  Then every term has at least three proper divisors, so \(S\) is defined for all indices.

**Claim.**  If \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then the sequence is strictly decreasing.

Proof of the claim.  
We examine two cases.

* \(a_1\) is odd.*  Then \(a_1\) has at least three proper divisors.  By Lemma 1, \(a_2=S(a_1)\) is odd.  The three smallest divisors of \(a_1\) are at least \(3,5,7\) (all odd), therefore  
\[
a_2 = \frac{a_1}{d_2}+\frac{a_1}{d_3}+\frac{a_1}{d_4} \le \frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{5}+\frac{a_1}{7} = \frac{71}{105}\,a_1 < a_1.
\]  
Hence \(a_2<a_1\).  Because \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\) (by Lemma 1 again, as \(a_1\) was not a multiple of \(6\) and \(S(a_1)\) is not a multiple of \(6\)), we can apply the same bound to \(a_2\) and get \(a_3<a_2\).  By induction the sequence is strictly decreasing.

* \(a_1\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\).*  Then \(a_1\) is even and \(3\nmid a_1\).  Write \(a_1=2^a\cdot m\) with \(m\) odd, \(a\ge 1\) and \(3\nmid m\).  We show directly that \(S(a_1)<a_1\).

– If \(a\ge 2\): then \(a_1\) is divisible by \(4\).  From Lemma 1 we know that \(S(a_1)\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and we also have the explicit expressions:  
  * for \(a\ge 3\): \(S(a_1)=\frac{7}{8}a_1\);  
  * for \(a=2\): \(S(a_1)=t\,(3p+4)\) with \(t=\frac{m}{p}\) odd, and \(a_1=4p\,t\); hence  
    \[
    S(a_1)=t\,(3p+4) \le t\cdot (4p-1) < 4p\,t = a_1.
    \]  
  In both situations \(S(a_1)<a_1\).

– If \(a=1\): then \(a_1=2m\) with \(m\) odd.  Using the analysis from Lemma 1 we obtain  
  \[
  S(a_1)=\begin{cases}
  t\,(p+3) & \text{if the third divisor is }2p,\\[2mm]
  v\,(pq+2p+2q) & \text{if the third divisor is }q<2p.
  \end{cases}
  \]  
  In the first case \(a_1=2p\,t\) and \(t\) is odd, so \(S(a_1)=t(p+3) < t\cdot2p = a_1\).  
  In the second case \(a_1=2pq\,v\) and \(v\) is odd, so \(S(a_1)=v\,(pq+2p+2q) < v\cdot(4p) \le v\cdot(4p) \le 2pq\,v = a_1\)?  Let's be precise: For \(p<q\) and \(q<2p\), we have \(pq+2p+2q < 4p\)? Actually we need a bound that shows \(S(a_1)<a_1\).  Since \(a_1=2pq\,v\), and \(S(a_1)=v\,(pq+2p+2q)\).  Compare \((pq+2p+2q)\) with \(2pq\).  Because \(q<2p\), we have \(2pq > 2p^2\)? Let's do direct:  
  \[
  a_1 - S(a_1) = 2pq\,v - v\,(pq+2p+2q) = v\,(pq - 2p - 2q) = v\,(p q - 2p - 2q).
  \]  
  But \(p q - 2p - 2q = (p-2)(q-2)-4\).  For \(p=5,q=7\), this is \(35-10-14=11\), positive.  For \(p=5,q=11\), \(55-10-22=23\).  So it's positive.  However, is it always positive?  Since \(q<2p\), we have \(p q - 2p - 2q = p(q-2) - 2q\).  For \(p\ge5\), \(q\ge p+?\) The smallest possible \(q\) is the next prime after \(p\).  For \(p=5\), \(q=7\) gives \(35-10-14=11>0\).  For \(p=7\), \(q=11\) gives \(77-14-22=41>0\).  So it's positive.  But we need a rigorous inequality.  We can note that because \(q\ge p+2\) (since both are odd primes), we have \(pq \ge p(p+2)=p^2+2p\).  Then \(pq-2p-2q \ge p^2+2p-2p-2q = p^2-2q\).  But \(p^2-2q\) might be negative for small \(p\)?  For \(p=5,q=7\), \(25-14=11>0\).  For \(p=7,q=11\), \(49-22=27>0\).  So it's positive.  However, we can also bound using the fact that \(q<2p\) implies \(2q<4p\), so \(pq-2p-2q \ge pq-2p-4p = pq-6p = p(q-6)\).  If \(p\ge5\) and \(q\ge p+2\), then \(q-6\) could be negative for \(p=5,q=7\) gives \(1\), positive.  For \(p=5,q=7\), \(p(q-6)=5*1=5>0\).  So that works.  But this is getting messy.  Actually we can avoid this by using the earlier comparison: \(S(a_1) = v\,(pq+2p+2q)\) and \(a_1 = 2pq\,v\).  Since \(pq+2p+2q < 2pq\) for all \(p,q\ge 3\)?  Let's check: \(2pq - (pq+2p+2q) = pq - 2p - 2q = (p-2)(q-2)-4\).  For \(p=5,q=7\), this is \(3*5-4=11>0\).  For \(p=3,q=5\), we get \(15-6-10=-1\), but note that \(p\) is the smallest odd prime factor of \(m\).  Since \(m\) is odd and \(3\nmid m\), the smallest odd prime factor is at least \(5\).  So \(p\ge5\).  Then \((p-2)(q-2)-4 \ge (3)(q-2)-4 = 3q-10\).  Since \(q\ge p+2\ge 7\), \(3q-10 \ge 11\).  So indeed it's positive.  So \(S(a_1) < a_1\).  That's rigorous enough.

Thus in all subcases when \(a_1\) is even and not a multiple of \(3\), we have \(S(a_1)<a_1\).

Now, by Lemma 1, \(S(a_1)\) is not a multiple of \(6\) (because \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\)).  Hence the same reasoning applies to \(S(a_1)\), giving \(S(S(a_1))<S(a_1)\), and so on.  Therefore the sequence is strictly decreasing.  ∎

We have shown that if \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then the sequence is strictly decreasing.  Moreover, all terms have at least three proper divisors, so the sequence is well‑defined for all indices.  Because it is strictly decreasing and consists of positive integers, there exists an index \(k\) such that \(a_k\le 6\).  Now we examine the possible values of \(a_k\):

– If \(a_k=6\), then \(a_k\) is a multiple of \(6\), contradicting the fact that all terms are not multiples of \(6\) (since the property “\(6\nmid N\)” is preserved by the recursion according to Lemma 1).  
– If \(a_k\le 5\), then \(a_k\) has at most two proper divisors: indeed, \(1\) is a proper divisor, and any number \(\le 5\) either is prime or is a square of a prime (like \(4\)), so it has fewer than three proper divisors, contradicting the hypothesis that every term has at least three proper divisors.

Thus the assumption that \(a_1\) is not a multiple of \(6\) leads to a contradiction.  Consequently any admissible sequence must satisfy \(6\mid a_1\).

---

### 3.  Characterisation of admissible \(a_1\)

Now we know that \(6\mid a_1\).  Let \(N = a_1\).  Write the prime factorisation of \(N\) as  

\[
N = 2^a \cdot 3^b \cdot c,
\]  

where \(c\) is odd, not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\), and \(a\ge 1,\ b\ge 1\).  

We determine when the recursively defined sequence stays inside the set of numbers having at least three proper divisors.

---

#### 3.1  The case \(a = 1\) ( \(N\) is even but not divisible by \(4\) )  

Then \(N = 2 \cdot 3^b \cdot c\) with \(c\) odd, not divisible by \(3\).  The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are  

\[
d_2 = 2,\quad d_3 = 3\ (\text{since }3\mid N),\quad d_4 = \begin{cases}
6 &\text{if }5\nmid N,\\
5 &\text{if }5\mid N.
\end{cases}
\]  

Indeed, if \(5\nmid N\) then the next divisor after \(3\) is \(6\) (because \(6\mid N\)), and \(5\) does not appear.  If \(5\mid N\) then \(5\) is a divisor and \(5<6\), so the third smallest divisor is \(5\).

Hence  

\[
S(N) = 
\begin{cases}
\displaystyle\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{6} = N, &\text{if }5\nmid N,\\[4mm]
\displaystyle\frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{5} = \frac{31}{30}\,N, &\text{if }5\mid N.
\end{cases}
\]

* If \(5\nmid N\), then \(a_2 = N\).  Thus the sequence is constant, and every term trivially has at least three proper divisors (since \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\)).  Therefore such \(N\) are admissible.

* If \(5\mid N\), then \(a_2 = \frac{31}{30}N\).  Because \(N\) is a multiple of \(30\) (it is a multiple of \(6\) and also of \(5\)), write \(N = 30\,s\).  Then \(a_2 = 31\,s\).  Note that \(a_2\) is odd.  Moreover, \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\) (by Lemma 1, since \(N\) is not a multiple of \(6\)? Wait \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\) in this case, but we are in the subcase \(5\mid N\), so \(N\) is a multiple of \(30\).  Then \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\).  However, we need to check whether the sequence can be good despite \(a_2\) not being a multiple of \(6\).  Since \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and has at least three proper divisors (it is part of the sequence), Lemma 1 tells us that \(a_3 = S(a_2)\) is not a multiple of \(6\) and satisfies \(a_3 < a_2\).  By induction the terms after \(a_2\) are strictly decreasing and never become multiples of \(6\).  Consequently we eventually reach a term \(\le 5\) that has fewer than three proper divisors, contradicting the hypothesis.  Hence such \(N\) cannot be admissible.

Thus for \(a=1\) the admissible values are exactly those multiples of \(6\) that are **not** multiples of \(5\).  (The condition \(5\nmid N\) is sufficient and necessary.)

---

#### 3.2  The case \(a \ge 2\) ( \(N\) is divisible by \(4\) )  

Here \(N\) is a multiple of \(12\) because it is a multiple of \(6\) and also of \(4\).  Write \(N = 12\,t\) with \(t\in\mathbb{N}\).  The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are \(2,3,4\) (since \(4\mid N\) and \(3\mid N\) and \(2\mid N\)).  Therefore  

\[
S(N) = \frac{N}{2}+\frac{N}{3}+\frac{N}{4} = \frac{13}{12}\,N = 13\,t.
\]

Thus \(a_2 = 13\,t\).

Now we examine whether \(a_2\) is a multiple of \(6\).  Because \(13\) is not divisible by \(2\) or \(3\), \(a_2\) is a multiple of \(6\) iff \(t\) is a multiple of \(6\).  If \(t\) is not a multiple of \(6\), then \(a_2\) is not a multiple of \(6\), and by Lemma 1 (applied to \(a_2\)) we deduce that the sequence from \(a_2\) onward is strictly decreasing and never becomes a multiple of \(6\).  As argued before, this eventually leads to a term \(\le5\) with fewer than three proper divisors, contradiction.  Therefore, for the sequence to be admissible we must
